<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Antarctica</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Continent</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about the continent. For the region, see <a href="/wiki/Antarctic" title="Antarctic">Antarctic</a>. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Antarctica_(disambiguation)" title="Antarctica (disambiguation)">Antarctica (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1066479718">.mw-parser-output .infobox-subbox{padding:0;border:none;margin:-3px;width:auto;min-width:100%;font-size:100%;clear:none;float:none;background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .infobox-3cols-child{margin:auto}.mw-parser-output .infobox .navbar{font-size:100%}body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-header,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-subheader,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-above,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-title,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-image,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-full-data,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-below{text-align:center}</style><table class="infobox"><caption class="infobox-title">Antarctica</caption><tbody><tr><td class="infobox-image" colspan="2"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Antarctica_(orthographic_projection).svg"><img alt="This map uses an orthographic projection, near-polar aspect. <div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Antarctica_6400px_from_Blue_Marble.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="6400" data-file-width="6400" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Antarctica_6400px_from_Blue_Marble.jpg/220px-Antarctica_6400px_from_Blue_Marble.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Antarctica_6400px_from_Blue_Marble.jpg/330px-Antarctica_6400px_from_Blue_Marble.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Antarctica_6400px_from_Blue_Marble.jpg/440px-Antarctica_6400px_from_Blue_Marble.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Antarctica_6400px_from_Blue_Marble.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Composite satellite image of Antarctica (2002)</div></div></div>
<p><b>Antarctica</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/æ/: 'a' in 'bad'">æ</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/ɑːr/: 'ar' in 'far'">ɑːr</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span></span>/</a></span> <span class="nowrap" style="font-size:85%">(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:En-us-Antarctica.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" decoding="async" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/></a></span><a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/57/En-us-Antarctica.ogg" title="En-us-Antarctica.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</span></span>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[note 1]</a></sup> is <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>'s southernmost and least-populated <a href="/wiki/Continent" title="Continent">continent</a>. Situated almost entirely south of the <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_Circle" title="Antarctic Circle">Antarctic Circle</a> and surrounded by the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Ocean" title="Southern Ocean">Southern Ocean</a>, it contains the geographic <a href="/wiki/South_Pole" title="South Pole">South Pole</a>. Antarctica is the fifth-largest continent, being nearly twice the size of <a href="/wiki/Australia_(continent)" title="Australia (continent)">Australia</a> and larger than Europe, and has an area of 14,200,000 km<sup>2</sup> (5,500,000 sq mi). Most of Antarctica is covered by ice, with an average thickness of 1.9 km (1.2 mi).
</p><p>Antarctica is on average the coldest, driest, and windiest of the continents, and it has the highest average <a href="/wiki/Elevation" title="Elevation">elevation</a>. It is mainly a <a href="/wiki/Polar_desert" title="Polar desert">polar desert</a>, with annual <a href="/wiki/Climate_of_Antarctica#Precipitation" title="Climate of Antarctica">precipitation</a> of over 200 mm (8 in) along the coast and far less inland. About 70% of the world's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freshwater" title="Freshwater">freshwater</a> reserves are frozen in Antarctica, which if melted would raise global <a href="/wiki/Sea_level" title="Sea level">sea levels</a> by almost 60 metres (200 ft). Antarctica holds the record for the <a href="/wiki/Lowest_temperature_recorded_on_Earth" title="Lowest temperature recorded on Earth">lowest measured temperature on Earth</a>, −89.2 °C (−128.6 °F). The coastal regions can reach temperatures over 10 °C (50 °F) in summer. Native <a href="/wiki/Wildlife_of_Antarctica" title="Wildlife of Antarctica">species of animals</a> include <a href="/wiki/Mite" title="Mite">mites</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nematode" title="Nematode">nematodes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Penguin" title="Penguin">penguins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pinniped" title="Pinniped">seals</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tardigrade" title="Tardigrade">tardigrades</a>. Where <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_flora" title="Antarctic flora">vegetation</a> occurs, it is mostly in the form of <a href="/wiki/Lichen" title="Lichen">lichen</a> or <a href="/wiki/Moss" title="Moss">moss</a>.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_ice_sheet" title="Antarctic ice sheet">ice shelves of Antarctica</a> were probably first seen in 1820, during <a href="/wiki/First_Russian_Antarctic_Expedition" title="First Russian Antarctic Expedition">a Russian expedition</a> led by <a href="/wiki/Fabian_Gottlieb_von_Bellingshausen" title="Fabian Gottlieb von Bellingshausen">Fabian Gottlieb von Bellingshausen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mikhail_Lazarev" title="Mikhail Lazarev">Mikhail Lazarev</a>. The decades that followed saw further <a href="/wiki/List_of_Antarctic_expeditions" title="List of Antarctic expeditions">exploration</a> in French, American, and British expeditions. The first confirmed landing was by a Norwegian team in 1895. In the early 20th century, there were a few expeditions into the interior of the continent. <a href="/wiki/Nimrod_Expedition" title="Nimrod Expedition">British explorers</a> were the first to reach the <a href="/wiki/South_magnetic_pole" title="South magnetic pole">magnetic South Pole</a> in 1909, and the geographic South Pole was first reached in 1911 by <a href="/wiki/Amundsen%27s_South_Pole_expedition" title="Amundsen's South Pole expedition">Norwegian explorers</a>.
</p><p>Antarctica is <a href="/wiki/Territorial_claims_in_Antarctica" title="Territorial claims in Antarctica">governed by about 30 countries</a>, all of which are parties of the 1959 <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_Treaty_System" title="Antarctic Treaty System">Antarctic Treaty System</a>. According to the terms of the treaty, military activity, mining, <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_explosion" title="Nuclear explosion">nuclear explosions</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_waste_disposal" title="Nuclear waste disposal">nuclear waste disposal</a> are all prohibited in Antarctica. <a href="/wiki/Tourism_in_Antarctica" title="Tourism in Antarctica">Tourism</a>, fishing and research are the main human activities in and around Antarctica. During the summer months, about 5,000 people reside at <a href="/wiki/Research_stations_in_Antarctica" title="Research stations in Antarctica">research stations</a>, a figure that drops to around 1,000 in the winter. Despite its remoteness, human activity has a significant impact on the continent via <a href="/wiki/Pollution" title="Pollution">pollution</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ozone_depletion" title="Ozone depletion">ozone depletion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_in_Antarctica" title="Climate change in Antarctica">climate change</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1657_map_Polus_Antarcticus.jpg"><img alt="17th century map of the Antarctic region" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4837" data-file-width="5500" decoding="async" height="193" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/1657_map_Polus_Antarcticus.jpg/220px-1657_map_Polus_Antarcticus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/1657_map_Polus_Antarcticus.jpg/330px-1657_map_Polus_Antarcticus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/1657_map_Polus_Antarcticus.jpg/440px-1657_map_Polus_Antarcticus.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1657_map_Polus_Antarcticus.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A speculative representation of Antarctica labelled as '<span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Terra Australis Incognita</i></span>' on <a href="/wiki/Jan_Janssonius" title="Jan Janssonius">Jan Janssonius</a>'s <i><span title="Dutch-language text"><i lang="nl">Zeekaart van het Zuidpoolgebied</i></span></i> (1657), <a href="/wiki/Het_Scheepvaartmuseum" title="Het Scheepvaartmuseum">Het Scheepvaartmuseum</a></div></div></div>
<p>The name given to the continent <a href="/wiki/Etymology" title="Etymology">originates</a> from the word <i>antarctic</i>, which comes from <a href="/wiki/Middle_French" title="Middle French">Middle French</a> <i><span title="Middle French (ca. 1400-1600)-language text"><i lang="frm">antartique</i></span></i> or <i><span title="Middle French (ca. 1400-1600)-language text"><i lang="frm">antarctique</i></span></i> ('opposite to the Arctic') and, in turn, the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> <i><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">antarcticus</i></span></i> ('opposite to the north'). <i><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Antarcticus</i></span></i> is derived from the <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> <span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el">ἀντι-</span></span> ('anti-') and <span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el">ἀρκτικός</span></span> ('of <a href="/wiki/Ursa_Major" title="Ursa Major">the Bear</a>', 'northern').<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OED_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-OED-5">[4]</a></sup> The Greek philosopher <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> wrote in <i><a href="/wiki/Meteorology_(Aristotle)" title="Meteorology (Aristotle)">Meteorology</a></i> about an "Antarctic region" in <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 350 BCE</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELettinck2021158_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELettinck2021158-6">[5]</a></sup> The Greek geographer <a href="/wiki/Marinus_of_Tyre" title="Marinus of Tyre">Marinus of Tyre</a> reportedly used the name in his world map from the second century CE, now lost. The <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Roman</a> authors <a href="/wiki/Gaius_Julius_Hyginus" title="Gaius Julius Hyginus">Hyginus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Apuleius" title="Apuleius">Apuleius</a> used for the South Pole the <a href="/wiki/Romanization" title="Romanization">romanised</a> Greek name <i>polus antarcticus,</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHyginus1992176_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHyginus1992176-7">[6]</a></sup> from which derived the <a href="/wiki/Old_French" title="Old French">Old French</a> <i>pole antartike</i> (modern <i>pôle antarctique</i>) attested in 1270, and from there the <a href="/wiki/Middle_English" title="Middle English">Middle English</a> <i>pol antartik</i>, found first in a <a href="/wiki/Treatise" title="Treatise">treatise</a> written by the English author <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Chaucer" title="Geoffrey Chaucer">Geoffrey Chaucer</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OED_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-OED-5">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>Until the discovery, belief by Europeans in the existence of a <i><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a href="/wiki/Terra_Australis" title="Terra Australis">Terra Australis</a></i></span></i>—a vast continent in the far south of the globe to balance the northern lands of Europe, Asia and North Africa—had existed as an intellectual concept since <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">classical antiquity</a>. The belief of such a land lasted until the <a href="/wiki/European_maritime_exploration_of_Australia" title="European maritime exploration of Australia">discovery of Australia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScottHiattMcIlroy20122–3_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScottHiattMcIlroy20122–3-8">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the early 19th century, explorer <a href="/wiki/Matthew_Flinders" title="Matthew Flinders">Matthew Flinders</a> doubted the existence of a detached continent south of Australia (then called <a href="/wiki/New_Holland_(Australia)" title="New Holland (Australia)">New Holland</a>), and thus advocated for the "Terra Australis" name to be used for Australia instead.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECawley2015130_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECawley2015130-9">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCroneMcPherson200975_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCroneMcPherson200975-10">[9]</a></sup> In 1824 the colonial authorities in <a href="/wiki/Sydney" title="Sydney">Sydney</a> officially renamed the continent of New Holland to Australia, leaving the term "Terra Australis" unavailable as a reference to Antarctica. Over the following decades, geographers used phrases such as "the Antarctic Continent". They searched for a more poetic replacement, suggesting names such as <i>Ultima</i> and <i>Antipodea</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECameron-Ash201820_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECameron-Ash201820-11">[10]</a></sup> <i>Antarctica</i> was adopted in the 1890s, with the first use of the name being attributed to the Scottish <a href="/wiki/Cartography" title="Cartography">cartographer</a> <a href="/wiki/John_George_Bartholomew" title="John George Bartholomew">John George Bartholomew</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NLS_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-NLS-12">[11]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Antarctica" title="Geography of Antarctica">Geography of Antarctica</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Extreme_points_of_Antarctica" title="Extreme points of Antarctica">Extreme points of Antarctica</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_mountains_in_Antarctica" title="List of mountains in Antarctica">List of mountains in Antarctica</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Antarctica.svg"><img alt="map of Antarctica" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="511" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="399" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Antarctica.svg/400px-Antarctica.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Antarctica.svg/600px-Antarctica.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Antarctica.svg/800px-Antarctica.svg.png 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Antarctica.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Antarctica" title="Eastern Antarctica">Eastern Antarctica</a> is to the right of the <a href="/wiki/Transantarctic_Mountains" title="Transantarctic Mountains">Transantarctic Mountains</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_Antarctica" title="Western Antarctica">Western Antarctica</a> is to the left.</div></div></div>
<p>Positioned asymmetrically around the South Pole and largely south of the <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_Circle" title="Antarctic Circle">Antarctic Circle</a> (one of the five major <a href="/wiki/Circle_of_latitude" title="Circle of latitude">circles of latitude</a> that mark maps of the world), Antarctica is surrounded by the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Ocean" title="Southern Ocean">Southern Ocean</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[note 2]</a></sup> Rivers exist in Antarctica, the longest being the <a href="/wiki/Onyx_River" title="Onyx River">Onyx</a>. Antarctica covers more than 14.2 million km<sup>2</sup> (5,500,000 sq mi), making it the fifth-largest continent, slightly less than 1.5 times the area of the United States. Its coastline is almost 18,000 km (11,200 mi) long:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIAfactbook-People_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-CIAfactbook-People-1">[1]</a></sup> As of 1983<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Antarctica&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, of the four coastal types, 44% of the coast is floating ice in the form of an <a href="/wiki/Ice_shelf" title="Ice shelf">ice shelf</a>, 38% consists of ice walls that rest on rock, 13% is <a href="/wiki/Ice_stream" title="Ice stream">ice streams</a> or the edge of glaciers, and the remaining 5% is exposed rock.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrewry1983_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrewry1983-15">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lakes" title="Lakes">lakes</a> that lie at the base of the continental <a href="/wiki/Ice_sheet" title="Ice sheet">ice sheet</a> occur mainly in the <a href="/wiki/McMurdo_Dry_Valleys" title="McMurdo Dry Valleys">McMurdo Dry Valleys</a> or various <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_oasis" title="Antarctic oasis">oases</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrewby2002115_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrewby2002115-16">[14]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Lake_Vostok" title="Lake Vostok">Lake Vostok</a>, discovered beneath Russia's <a href="/wiki/Vostok_Station" title="Vostok Station">Vostok Station</a>, is the largest <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sub-glacial_lake" title="Sub-glacial lake">sub-glacial lake</a> globally, and one of the largest lakes in the world. It was once believed that the lake had been sealed off for millions of years, but scientists now estimate its water is replaced by the slow melting and freezing of ice caps every 13,000 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDay2019Is_all_of_Antarctica_snow-covered?_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDay2019Is_all_of_Antarctica_snow-covered?-17">[15]</a></sup> During the summer, the ice at the edges of the lakes can melt, and liquid moats temporarily form. Antarctica has both <a href="/wiki/Salt_lake" title="Salt lake">saline</a> and freshwater lakes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrewby2002115_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrewby2002115-16">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>Antarctica is divided into <a href="/wiki/West_Antarctica" title="West Antarctica">West Antarctica</a> and <a href="/wiki/East_Antarctica" title="East Antarctica">East Antarctica</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Transantarctic_Mountains" title="Transantarctic Mountains">Transantarctic Mountains</a>, which stretch from <a href="/wiki/Victoria_Land" title="Victoria Land">Victoria Land</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Ross_Sea" title="Ross Sea">Ross Sea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarrollLopes201999_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarrollLopes201999-18">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[17]</a></sup> The vast majority of Antarctica is covered by the <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_ice_sheet" title="Antarctic ice sheet">Antarctic ice sheet</a>, which averages 1.9 km (1.2 mi) in thickness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bedmap2_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bedmap2-20">[18]</a></sup> The ice sheet extends to all but a few <a href="/wiki/Oasis" title="Oasis">oases</a>, which, with the exception of the McMurdo Dry Valleys, are located in coastal areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[19]</a></sup> Several Antarctic ice streams flow to one of the many <a href="/wiki/Ice_shelf#Antarctic_ice_shelves" title="Ice shelf">Antarctic ice shelves</a>, a process described by <a href="/wiki/Ice-sheet_dynamics" title="Ice-sheet dynamics">ice-sheet dynamics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[20]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mount_Vinson_from_NW_at_Vinson_Plateau_by_Christian_Stangl_(flickr).jpg"><img alt="photograp of Vinson Massif" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="991" data-file-width="1400" decoding="async" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Mount_Vinson_from_NW_at_Vinson_Plateau_by_Christian_Stangl_%28flickr%29.jpg/220px-Mount_Vinson_from_NW_at_Vinson_Plateau_by_Christian_Stangl_%28flickr%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Mount_Vinson_from_NW_at_Vinson_Plateau_by_Christian_Stangl_%28flickr%29.jpg/330px-Mount_Vinson_from_NW_at_Vinson_Plateau_by_Christian_Stangl_%28flickr%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Mount_Vinson_from_NW_at_Vinson_Plateau_by_Christian_Stangl_%28flickr%29.jpg/440px-Mount_Vinson_from_NW_at_Vinson_Plateau_by_Christian_Stangl_%28flickr%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mount_Vinson_from_NW_at_Vinson_Plateau_by_Christian_Stangl_(flickr).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Vinson_Massif" title="Vinson Massif">Vinson Massif</a> from the northwest, the highest peak in Antarctica</div></div></div>
<p>East Antarctica comprises <a href="/wiki/Coats_Land" title="Coats Land">Coats Land</a>, <a href="/wiki/Queen_Maud_Land" title="Queen Maud Land">Queen Maud Land</a>, <a href="/wiki/Enderby_Land" title="Enderby Land">Enderby Land</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mac._Robertson_Land" title="Mac. Robertson Land"><abbr title="Macpherson">Mac</abbr>. Robertson Land</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wilkes_Land" title="Wilkes Land">Wilkes Land</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Victoria_Land" title="Victoria Land">Victoria Land</a>. All but a small portion of the region lies within the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Hemisphere" title="Eastern Hemisphere">Eastern Hemisphere</a>. East Antarctica is largely covered by the <a href="/wiki/East_Antarctic_Ice_Sheet" title="East Antarctic Ice Sheet">East Antarctic Ice Sheet</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESiegertFlorindo2008532_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESiegertFlorindo2008532-23">[21]</a></sup> There are numerous <a href="/wiki/List_of_Antarctic_and_subantarctic_islands" title="List of Antarctic and subantarctic islands">islands</a> surrounding Antarctica, most of which are volcanic and very young by geological standards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-O&amp;E_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-O&amp;E-24">[22]</a></sup> The most prominent exceptions to this are the islands of the <a href="/wiki/Kerguelen_Plateau" title="Kerguelen Plateau">Kerguelen Plateau</a>, the earliest of which formed around 40 <a href="/wiki/Year#SI_prefix_multipliers" title="Year">Ma</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-O&amp;E_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-O&amp;E-24">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Vinson_Massif" title="Vinson Massif">Vinson Massif</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/Ellsworth_Mountains" title="Ellsworth Mountains">Ellsworth Mountains</a>, is the highest peak in Antarctica at 4,892 m (16,050 ft).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMonteath1997135_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMonteath1997135-26">[24]</a></sup> Mount Erebus on <a href="/wiki/Ross_Island" title="Ross Island">Ross Island</a> is the world's southernmost active <a href="/wiki/Volcano" title="Volcano">volcano</a> and <a href="/wiki/Types_of_volcanic_eruptions" title="Types of volcanic eruptions">erupts</a> around 10 times each day. Ash from eruptions has been found 300 kilometres (190 mi) from the <a href="/wiki/Volcanic_crater" title="Volcanic crater">volcanic crater</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrewby200275_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrewby200275-27">[25]</a></sup> There is evidence of a large number of volcanoes under the ice, which could pose a risk to the ice sheet if activity levels were to rise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarrollLopes201938_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarrollLopes201938-28">[26]</a></sup> The ice dome known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dome_Argus" title="Dome Argus">Dome Argus</a> in East Antarctica is the highest Antarctic ice feature, at 4,091 metres (13,422 ft). It is one of the world's coldest and driest places—temperatures there may reach as low as −90 °C (−130 °F), and the annual precipitation is 1–3 cm (0.39–1.18 in).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHund2014362–363_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHund2014362–363-29">[27]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geologic_history">Geologic history</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geology_of_Antarctica" title="Geology of Antarctica">Geology of Antarctica</a></div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Geology_of_the_Antarctic_Peninsula" title="Geology of the Antarctic Peninsula">Geology of the Antarctic Peninsula</a></div>
<p>From the end of the <a href="/wiki/Neoproterozoic" title="Neoproterozoic">Neoproterozoic</a> era to the <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous" title="Cretaceous">Cretaceous</a>, Antarctica was part of the <a href="/wiki/Supercontinent" title="Supercontinent">supercontinent</a> <a href="/wiki/Gondwana" title="Gondwana">Gondwana</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[28]</a></sup> Modern Antarctica was formed as Gondwana gradually broke apart beginning around 183 Ma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrewby200292_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrewby200292-31">[29]</a></sup> For a large proportion of the <a href="/wiki/Phanerozoic" title="Phanerozoic">Phanerozoic</a>, Antarctica had a <a href="/wiki/Tropical_climate" title="Tropical climate">tropical</a> or <a href="/wiki/Temperate_climate" title="Temperate climate">temperate climate</a>, and it was covered in forests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Palaeozoic_era_.28540.E2.80.93250_Ma.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Palaeozoic_era_(540–250_Ma)">Palaeozoic era (540–250 Ma)</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Glossopteris_sp._(fossil_leaf)_(Permian;_Antarctica)_1_(49063572172)_(cropped).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="998" data-file-width="2625" decoding="async" height="84" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Glossopteris_sp._%28fossil_leaf%29_%28Permian%3B_Antarctica%29_1_%2849063572172%29_%28cropped%29.jpg/220px-Glossopteris_sp._%28fossil_leaf%29_%28Permian%3B_Antarctica%29_1_%2849063572172%29_%28cropped%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Glossopteris_sp._%28fossil_leaf%29_%28Permian%3B_Antarctica%29_1_%2849063572172%29_%28cropped%29.jpg/330px-Glossopteris_sp._%28fossil_leaf%29_%28Permian%3B_Antarctica%29_1_%2849063572172%29_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Glossopteris_sp._%28fossil_leaf%29_%28Permian%3B_Antarctica%29_1_%2849063572172%29_%28cropped%29.jpg/440px-Glossopteris_sp._%28fossil_leaf%29_%28Permian%3B_Antarctica%29_1_%2849063572172%29_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Glossopteris_sp._(fossil_leaf)_(Permian;_Antarctica)_1_(49063572172)_(cropped).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Glossopteris" title="Glossopteris">Glossopteris</a></i> sp. leaf from the Permian of Antarctica</div></div></div>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/Cambrian" title="Cambrian">Cambrian period</a>, Gondwana had a mild climate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECantrillPoole201231_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECantrillPoole201231-33">[31]</a></sup> West Antarctica was partially in the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Hemisphere" title="Northern Hemisphere">Northern Hemisphere</a>, and during the time, large amounts of <a href="/wiki/Sandstone" title="Sandstone">sandstones</a>, <a href="/wiki/Limestone" title="Limestone">limestones</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Shale" title="Shale">shales</a> were deposited. East Antarctica was at the equator, where seafloor <a href="/wiki/Invertebrate" title="Invertebrate">invertebrates</a> and <a href="/wiki/Trilobite" title="Trilobite">trilobites</a> flourished in the tropical seas. By the start of the <a href="/wiki/Devonian" title="Devonian">Devonian period</a> (416 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Annum" title="Annum">Ma</a>), Gondwana was in more southern latitudes and the climate was cooler, though fossils of land plants are known from then. Sand and <a href="/wiki/Silt" title="Silt">silts</a> were laid down in what is now the Ellsworth, <a href="/wiki/Horlick_Mountains" title="Horlick Mountains">Horlick</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pensacola_Mountains" title="Pensacola Mountains">Pensacola Mountains</a>.
</p><p>Antarctica became glaciated during the <a href="/wiki/Late_Paleozoic_icehouse" title="Late Paleozoic icehouse">Late Paleozoic icehouse</a> beginning at the end of the Devonian period (360 Ma), though glaciation would substantially increase during the late <a href="/wiki/Carboniferous" title="Carboniferous">Carboniferous</a>. It drifted closer to the South Pole and the climate cooled, though <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_flora" title="Antarctic flora">flora</a> remained.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[32]</a></sup> After deglaciation during the latter half of the Early <a href="/wiki/Permian" title="Permian">Permian</a>, the land became dominated by <a href="/wiki/Glossopteridales" title="Glossopteridales">glossopterids</a> (an extinct group of seed plants with no close living relatives), most prominently <i><a href="/wiki/Glossopteris" title="Glossopteris">Glossopteris</a>,</i> a tree interpreted as growing in waterlogged soils, which formed extensive coal deposits. Other plants found in Antarctica during the Permian include <a href="/wiki/Cordaitales" title="Cordaitales">Cordaitales</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sphenopsid" title="Sphenopsid">sphenopsids</a>, ferns, and <a href="/wiki/Lycophyte" title="Lycophyte">lycophytes</a>.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTECantrillPoole201257–104"Collapsing_ice_sheets_and_evolving_polar_forests_of_the_middle_to_late_Paleozoic"_35-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTECantrillPoole201257–104"Collapsing_ice_sheets_and_evolving_polar_forests_of_the_middle_to_late_Paleozoic"-35'>[33]</a></sup> At the end of the Permian the climate became drier and hotter over much of Gondwana and the glossopterid forest ecosystems collapsed, as part of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/End-Permian_mass_extinction" title="End-Permian mass extinction">End-Permian mass extinction</a>.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTECantrillPoole201257–104"Collapsing_ice_sheets_and_evolving_polar_forests_of_the_middle_to_late_Paleozoic"_35-1'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTECantrillPoole201257–104"Collapsing_ice_sheets_and_evolving_polar_forests_of_the_middle_to_late_Paleozoic"-35'>[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[34]</a></sup> There is no evidence of any <a href="/wiki/Tetrapod" title="Tetrapod">tetrapods</a> having lived in Antarctica during the Paleozoic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-:4-37">[35]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Mesozoic_era_.28250.E2.80.9366_Ma.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Mesozoic_era_(250–66_Ma)">Mesozoic era (250–66 Ma)</span></h3>
<p>The continued warming dried out much of Gondwana. During the Triassic, Antarctica was dominated by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seed_fern" title="Seed fern">seed ferns</a> (pteridosperms) belonging to the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Dicroidium" title="Dicroidium">Dicroidium</a>,</i> which grew as trees. Other associated Triassic flora included <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ginkgophyte" title="Ginkgophyte">ginkgophytes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cycad" title="Cycad">cycadophytes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Conifer" title="Conifer">conifers</a>, and sphenopsids.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTECantrillPoole2012105–160"Icehouse_to_hothouse:_floral_turnover,_the_Permian–Triassic_crisis_and_Triassic_vegetation"_38-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTECantrillPoole2012105–160"Icehouse_to_hothouse:_floral_turnover,_the_Permian–Triassic_crisis_and_Triassic_vegetation"-38'>[36]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Tetrapod" title="Tetrapod">Tetrapods</a> first appeared in Antarctica during the early <a href="/wiki/Triassic" title="Triassic">Triassic</a>, with the earliest known fossils found in the <a href="/wiki/Fremouw_Formation" title="Fremouw Formation">Fremouw Formation</a> of the Transantarctic Mountains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-:4-37">[35]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Synapsid" title="Synapsid">Synapsids</a> (also known as "mammal-like reptiles") included species such as <i><a href="/wiki/Lystrosaurus" title="Lystrosaurus">Lystrosaurus</a></i>, and were common during the <a href="/wiki/Early_Triassic" title="Early Triassic">Early Triassic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJasinoski2013139_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJasinoski2013139-39">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_Peninsula" title="Antarctic Peninsula">Antarctic Peninsula</a> began to form during the <a href="/wiki/Jurassic" title="Jurassic">Jurassic</a> period (206–146 Ma).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[38]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Ginkgo" title="Ginkgo">Ginkgo</a></i> trees, conifers, <a href="/wiki/Bennettitales" title="Bennettitales">Bennettitales</a>, <a href="/wiki/Equisetum" title="Equisetum">horsetails</a>, ferns and cycads were plentiful during the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECantrillPoole20129,_35,_56,_71,_185,_314_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECantrillPoole20129,_35,_56,_71,_185,_314-41">[39]</a></sup> In West Antarctica, coniferous forests dominated throughout the Cretaceous period (146–66 Ma), though southern beech trees (<i><a href="/wiki/Nothofagus" title="Nothofagus">Nothofagus</a></i>) became prominent towards the end of the Cretaceous.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERiffenburgh2007413_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERiffenburgh2007413-43">[41]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ammonite" title="Ammonite">Ammonites</a> were common in the seas around Antarctica, and dinosaurs were also present, though only a few Antarctic dinosaur <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genera" title="Genera">genera</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Cryolophosaurus" title="Cryolophosaurus">Cryolophosaurus</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Glacialisaurus" title="Glacialisaurus">Glacialisaurus</a></i>, from the Early Jurassic <a href="/wiki/Hanson_Formation" title="Hanson Formation">Hanson Formation</a> of the Transantarctic Mountains,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SmithPol2007_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-SmithPol2007-44">[42]</a></sup> and <i><a href="/wiki/Antarctopelta" title="Antarctopelta">Antarctopelta</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Trinisaura" title="Trinisaura">Trinisaura</a>,</i> <i><a href="/wiki/Morrosaurus" title="Morrosaurus">Morrosaurus</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Imperobator" title="Imperobator">Imperobator</a></i> from Late Cretaceous of the Antarctic Peninsula) have been described.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trinisaura_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Trinisaura-45">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morrosaurus_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Morrosaurus-46">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ely&amp;Case2019_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ely&amp;Case2019-47">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Gondwana_breakup_.28160.E2.80.9315_Ma.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Gondwana_breakup_(160–15_Ma)">Gondwana breakup (160–15 Ma)</span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .multiimageinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{display:flex;flex-direction:row;clear:left;flex-wrap:wrap;width:100%;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{margin:1px;float:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{justify-content:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle .thumbcaption{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow>.thumbcaption{text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:152px;max-width:152px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Opening_of_South_Atlantic_126_Ma.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="798" data-file-width="798" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Opening_of_South_Atlantic_126_Ma.png/150px-Opening_of_South_Atlantic_126_Ma.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Opening_of_South_Atlantic_126_Ma.png/225px-Opening_of_South_Atlantic_126_Ma.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Opening_of_South_Atlantic_126_Ma.png/300px-Opening_of_South_Atlantic_126_Ma.png 2x" width="150"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:152px;max-width:152px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Opening_of_South_Atlantic_83_Ma.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="798" data-file-width="798" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Opening_of_South_Atlantic_83_Ma.png/150px-Opening_of_South_Atlantic_83_Ma.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Opening_of_South_Atlantic_83_Ma.png/225px-Opening_of_South_Atlantic_83_Ma.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Opening_of_South_Atlantic_83_Ma.png/300px-Opening_of_South_Atlantic_83_Ma.png 2x" width="150"/></a></div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption">Breakup of Gondwana at <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 150</span>  Ma (<i>left</i>), c. 126 Ma (<i>centre</i>) and at c. 83 Ma (<i>right</i>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[note 3]</a></sup></div></div></div></div>
<p>Africa separated from Antarctica in the Jurassic around 160 Ma, followed by the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a> in the early Cretaceous (about 125 Ma).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[47]</a></sup> During the early <a href="/wiki/Paleogene" title="Paleogene">Paleogene</a>, Antarctica remained connected to South America via the Isthmus of Scotia as well as to southeastern Australia. Fauna from the <a href="/wiki/La_Meseta_Formation" title="La Meseta Formation">La Meseta Formation</a> in the Antarctic Peninsula, dating to the <a href="/wiki/Eocene" title="Eocene">Eocene</a>, is very similar to equivalent South American faunas; with <a href="/wiki/Marsupial" title="Marsupial">marsupials</a>, <a href="/wiki/Xenarthra" title="Xenarthra">xenarthrans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Litopterna" title="Litopterna">litoptern</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Astrapotheria" title="Astrapotheria">astrapotherian</a> <a href="/wiki/Ungulate" title="Ungulate">ungulates</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Gondwanatheria" title="Gondwanatheria">gondwanatheres</a> and <a href="/wiki/Meridiolestida" title="Meridiolestida">meridiolestidans</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[49]</a></sup> Marsupials are thought to have dispersed into Australia via Antarctica by the early Eocene.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>Around 53 Ma, Australia-<a href="/wiki/New_Guinea" title="New Guinea">New Guinea</a> separated from Antarctica, opening the <a href="/wiki/Tasmanian_Passage" title="Tasmanian Passage">Tasmanian Passage</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[51]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Drake_Passage" title="Drake Passage">Drake Passage</a> opened between Antarctica and South America around 30 Ma, resulting in the creation of the <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_Circumpolar_Current" title="Antarctic Circumpolar Current">Antarctic Circumpolar Current</a> that completely isolated the continent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[52]</a></sup> Models of Antarctic geography suggest that this current, as well as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Feedback_loop" title="Feedback loop">feedback loop</a> caused by lowering <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">CO<sub>2</sub></a> levels, caused the creation of small yet permanent polar ice caps. As CO<sub>2</sub> levels declined further the ice began to spread rapidly, replacing the forests that until then had covered Antarctica.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DeContoPollard2003_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-DeContoPollard2003-56">[53]</a></sup> Since about 15 Ma, the continent has been mostly covered with ice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrewby2002102_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrewby2002102-57">[54]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Present_day">Present day</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:392px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Antarctic_tectonic_plate.svg"><img alt="map of the tectonic plates of the southern hemisphere" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="922" data-file-width="1381" decoding="async" height="260" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/The_Antarctic_tectonic_plate.svg/390px-The_Antarctic_tectonic_plate.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/The_Antarctic_tectonic_plate.svg/585px-The_Antarctic_tectonic_plate.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/The_Antarctic_tectonic_plate.svg/780px-The_Antarctic_tectonic_plate.svg.png 2x" width="390"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Antarctic_tectonic_plate.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_Plate" title="Antarctic Plate">Antarctic Plate</a></div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Geology" title="Geology">geology</a> of Antarctica, largely obscured by the continental ice sheet,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrewby200288_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrewby200288-58">[55]</a></sup> is being revealed by techniques such as <a href="/wiki/Remote_sensing" title="Remote sensing">remote sensing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ground-penetrating_radar" title="Ground-penetrating radar">ground-penetrating radar</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Satellite_imagery" title="Satellite imagery">satellite imagery</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pou_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pou-59">[56]</a></sup> Geologically, West Antarctica closely resembles the South American <a href="/wiki/Andes" title="Andes">Andes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStonehouse2002116_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStonehouse2002116-60">[57]</a></sup> The Antarctic Peninsula was formed by <a href="/wiki/Orogeny" title="Orogeny">geologic uplift</a> and the transformation of sea bed <a href="/wiki/Sediment" title="Sediment">sediments</a> into <a href="/wiki/Metamorphic_rock" title="Metamorphic rock">metamorphic rocks</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fel_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fel-61">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p>West Antarctica was formed by the merging of several <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Continental_plates" title="Continental plates">continental plates</a>, which created a number of mountain ranges in the region, the most prominent being the Ellsworth Mountains. The presence of the <a href="/wiki/West_Antarctic_Rift_System" title="West Antarctic Rift System">West Antarctic Rift System</a> has resulted in <a href="/wiki/Volcanism" title="Volcanism">volcanism</a> along the border between West and East Antarctica, as well as the creation of the Transantarctic Mountains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrewby2002144,_197–198_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrewby2002144,_197–198-62">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>East Antarctica is geologically varied. Its formation began during the <a href="/wiki/Archean" title="Archean">Archean</a> Eon (4,000 Ma–2,500 Ma), and stopped during the Cambrian Period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnderson201028_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnderson201028-63">[60]</a></sup> It is built on a <a href="/wiki/Craton" title="Craton">craton</a> of rock, which is the basis of the Precambrian <a href="/wiki/Shield_(geology)" title="Shield (geology)">Shield</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrewby200271_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrewby200271-64">[61]</a></sup> On top of the base are <a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">coal</a> and sandstones, limestones, and shales that were laid down during the Devonian and Jurassic periods to form the Transantarctic Mountains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbellClaridge1987_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbellClaridge1987-65">[62]</a></sup> In coastal areas such as the <a href="/wiki/Shackleton_Range" title="Shackleton Range">Shackleton Range</a> and Victoria Land, some <a href="/wiki/Fault_(geology)" title="Fault (geology)">faulting</a> has occurred.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>Coal was first recorded in Antarctica near the <a href="/wiki/Beardmore_Glacier" title="Beardmore Glacier">Beardmore Glacier</a> by <a href="/wiki/Frank_Wild" title="Frank Wild">Frank Wild</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Nimrod_Expedition" title="Nimrod Expedition"><i>Nimrod</i> Expedition</a> in 1907, and low-grade coal is known to exist across many parts of the Transantarctic Mountains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrewby2002124_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrewby2002124-68">[65]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Prince_Charles_Mountains" title="Prince Charles Mountains">Prince Charles Mountains</a> contain deposits of <a href="/wiki/Iron_ore" title="Iron ore">iron ore</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sul_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sul-69">[66]</a></sup> There are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oil_field" title="Oil field">oil</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natural_gas_field" title="Natural gas field">natural gas fields</a> in the Ross Sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[67]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Climate_of_Antarctica" title="Climate of Antarctica">Climate of Antarctica</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:342px;max-width:342px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:175px;max-width:175px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:120px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fryxellsee_Opt.jpg"><img alt="photograph of blue ice" data-file-height="1083" data-file-width="1560" decoding="async" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Fryxellsee_Opt.jpg/173px-Fryxellsee_Opt.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Fryxellsee_Opt.jpg/260px-Fryxellsee_Opt.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Fryxellsee_Opt.jpg/346px-Fryxellsee_Opt.jpg 2x" width="173"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><a href="/wiki/Blue_ice_(glacial)" title="Blue ice (glacial)">Blue ice</a> covering <a href="/wiki/Lake_Fryxell" title="Lake Fryxell">Lake Fryxell</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/Transantarctic_Mountains" title="Transantarctic Mountains">Transantarctic Mountains</a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:163px;max-width:163px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:120px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AntarcticaSummer.jpg"><img alt="Two men looking at a penguin on a sunny day" data-file-height="1063" data-file-width="1417" decoding="async" height="121" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/AntarcticaSummer.jpg/161px-AntarcticaSummer.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/AntarcticaSummer.jpg/242px-AntarcticaSummer.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/AntarcticaSummer.jpg/322px-AntarcticaSummer.jpg 2x" width="161"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Temperate conditions near the coast in December</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>Antarctica is the coldest, windiest, and driest of Earth's continents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIAfactbook-People_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-CIAfactbook-People-1">[1]</a></sup> The lowest natural air temperature ever recorded on Earth was −89.2 °C (−128.6 °F) at the Russian Vostok Station in Antarctica on 21 July 1983.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[68]</a></sup> A lower air temperature of −94.7 °C (−138.5 °F) was recorded in 2010 by satellite—however, it may have been influenced by ground temperatures and was not recorded at a height of 2 m (7 ft) above the surface as required for official air temperature records.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ric_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ric-72">[69]</a></sup> Average temperatures can reach a minimum of between −80 °C (−112 °F) in the interior of the continent during winter and a maximum of over 10 °C (50 °F) near the coast in summer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[70]</a></sup>
</p><p>Antarctica is a <a href="/wiki/Polar_desert" title="Polar desert">polar desert</a> with little <a href="/wiki/Precipitation" title="Precipitation">precipitation</a>; the continent receives an average equivalent to about 150 mm (6 in) of water per year, mostly in the form of snow. The interior is dryer, and receives less than 50 mm (2 in) per year, whereas the coastal regions typically receive more than 200 mm (8 in).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[71]</a></sup> In a few <a href="/wiki/Blue-ice_area" title="Blue-ice area">blue-ice areas</a>, the wind and <a href="/wiki/Sublimation_(phase_transition)" title="Sublimation (phase transition)">sublimation</a> remove more snow than is accumulated by precipitation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[72]</a></sup> In the dry valleys, the same effect occurs over a rock base, leading to a barren and <a href="/wiki/Desiccation" title="Desiccation">desiccated</a> landscape.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[73]</a></sup> Antarctica is colder than the <a href="/wiki/Arctic" title="Arctic">Arctic</a> region, as much of Antarctica is over 3,000 m (9,800 ft) above sea level, where air temperatures are colder. The relative warmth of the <a href="/wiki/Arctic_Ocean" title="Arctic Ocean">Arctic Ocean</a> is transferred through the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arctic_sea_ice" title="Arctic sea ice">Arctic sea ice</a> and moderates temperatures in the Arctic region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERohliVega2018241_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERohliVega2018241-77">[74]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Regional_differences">Regional differences</span></h3>
<p>East Antarctica is colder than its western counterpart because of its higher elevation. <a href="/wiki/Weather_front" title="Weather front">Weather fronts</a> rarely penetrate far into the continent, leaving the centre cold and dry, with moderate wind speeds. Heavy snowfalls are common on the coastal portion of Antarctica, where snowfalls of up to 1.22 m (48 in) in 48 hours have been recorded. At the continent's edge, strong <a href="/wiki/Katabatic_wind" title="Katabatic wind">katabatic winds</a> off of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polar_plateau" title="Polar plateau">polar plateau</a> often blow at <a href="/wiki/Storm#Classification" title="Storm">storm force</a>. During the summer, more <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_radiation" title="Solar radiation">solar radiation</a> reaches the surface at the South Pole than at the <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">equator</a>, because of the 24 hours of sunlight received there each day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIAfactbook-People_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-CIAfactbook-People-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate_change">Climate change<span class="anchor" id="Effects_of_global_warming"></span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_in_Antarctica" title="Climate change in Antarctica">Climate change in Antarctica</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:234px;max-width:234px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:232px;max-width:232px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AntarcticaTemps_1957-2006.jpg"><img alt="Warming in West Antarctica was up to 0.25 degrees Celsius, whereas East Antarctica saw more minor temperature rise" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="720" decoding="async" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/AntarcticaTemps_1957-2006.jpg/230px-AntarcticaTemps_1957-2006.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/AntarcticaTemps_1957-2006.jpg/345px-AntarcticaTemps_1957-2006.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/AntarcticaTemps_1957-2006.jpg/460px-AntarcticaTemps_1957-2006.jpg 2x" width="230"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">The warming trend for Antarctica from 1957 to 2006, based on the analysis of <a href="/wiki/Weather_station" title="Weather station">weather station</a> and <a href="/wiki/Weather_satellite" title="Weather satellite">satellite</a> data; dark tints over West Antarctica indicates that the region warmed most per decade.</div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:232px;max-width:232px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Scale_for_chart_(Antarctica_warming_1957-2006).svg"><img alt="Legend" data-file-height="115" data-file-width="736" decoding="async" height="36" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Scale_for_chart_%28Antarctica_warming_1957-2006%29.svg/230px-Scale_for_chart_%28Antarctica_warming_1957-2006%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Scale_for_chart_%28Antarctica_warming_1957-2006%29.svg/345px-Scale_for_chart_%28Antarctica_warming_1957-2006%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Scale_for_chart_%28Antarctica_warming_1957-2006%29.svg/460px-Scale_for_chart_%28Antarctica_warming_1957-2006%29.svg.png 2x" width="230"/></a></div></div></div></div></div>
<p>Over the second half of the 20th century, the Antarctic Peninsula was the fastest-warming place on Earth, closely followed by West Antarctica, but temperatures rose less rapidly during the early 21st century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[75]</a></sup> Conversely, the South Pole, located in East Antarctica, barely warmed during much of the 20th century, but temperatures rose three times the global average between 1990 and 2020.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[76]</a></sup> In February 2020, the continent recorded its highest temperature of 18.3 °C (64.9 °F), which was 0.8 °C (1.4 °F) higher than the previous record attained in March 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC20_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC20-80">[77]</a></sup>
</p><p>There is some evidence that surface <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">warming</a> in Antarctica is due to human <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gas emissions</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gillett2008_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gillett2008-81">[78]</a></sup> but it is difficult to determine due to <a href="/wiki/Climate_variability_and_change" title="Climate variability and change">internal variability</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Steig2013_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Steig2013-82">[79]</a></sup> A main component of climate variability in Antarctica is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southern_Annular_Mode" title="Southern Annular Mode">Southern Annular Mode</a> (a low-frequency <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mode_of_variability" title="Mode of variability">mode of atmospheric variability</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Hemisphere" title="Southern Hemisphere">Southern Hemisphere</a>), which showed strengthened winds around Antarctica in the summer of the later decades of the 20th century, associated with cooler temperatures over the continent. The trend was at a scale unprecedented over the last 600 years; the most dominant driver of the mode of variability is likely the <a href="/wiki/Ozone_depletion" title="Ozone depletion">depletion of ozone</a> above the continent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[80]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Glaciers_and_floating_ice">Glaciers and floating ice</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Sea_level_rise" title="Sea level rise">Sea level rise</a> and <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_sea_ice" title="Antarctic sea ice">Antarctic sea ice</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pine_Island_Glacier_-_NASA_satellite_image_Nov_2011.jpg"><img alt="photograph of a glacier ice shelf" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3479" data-file-width="2952" decoding="async" height="259" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Pine_Island_Glacier_-_NASA_satellite_image_Nov_2011.jpg/220px-Pine_Island_Glacier_-_NASA_satellite_image_Nov_2011.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Pine_Island_Glacier_-_NASA_satellite_image_Nov_2011.jpg/330px-Pine_Island_Glacier_-_NASA_satellite_image_Nov_2011.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Pine_Island_Glacier_-_NASA_satellite_image_Nov_2011.jpg/440px-Pine_Island_Glacier_-_NASA_satellite_image_Nov_2011.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pine_Island_Glacier_-_NASA_satellite_image_Nov_2011.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Pine_Island_Glacier" title="Pine Island Glacier">Pine Island Glacier</a>, photographed in November 2011</div></div></div>
<p>Precipitation in Antarctica occurs in the form of snow, which accumulates and forms the giant ice sheet that covers the continent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThomas200724_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThomas200724-84">[81]</a></sup> Under the force of gravity, the ice flows towards the coast. The ice then moves into the ocean, often forming vast floating <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ice_shelves" title="Ice shelves">ice shelves</a>. These shelves can melt or form <a href="/wiki/Iceberg" title="Iceberg">icebergs</a> that eventually disintegrate when they reach warmer ocean waters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThomas200726_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThomas200726-85">[82]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sea_ice_and_ice_shelves">Sea ice and ice shelves</span></h3>
<p>Sea ice extent expands annually during the Antarctic winter, but most of it melts in the summer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[83]</a></sup> The ice is formed from the ocean, and does not contribute to changes in sea level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[84]</a></sup> The average extent of sea ice around Antarctica has changed little since satellites began to observe the Earth's surface in 1978; which is in contrast with the Arctic, where there has been rapid sea ice loss. A possible explanation is that <a href="/wiki/Thermohaline_circulation" title="Thermohaline circulation">thermohaline circulation</a> transports warmed water to deeper layers in the Southern Ocean, so that the surface remains relatively cool.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p>The melting of the ice shelves does not contribute much to sea level rise, as the floating ice displaces its own mass of water, but the ice shelves act to stabilize the land ice. They are vulnerable to warming water, which has caused large ice shelves to collapse into the ocean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RignotCasassa2004_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-RignotCasassa2004-89">[86]</a></sup> The loss of ice shelf "buttressing" has been identified as the major cause of ice loss on the West Antarctic ice sheet, but has also been observed around the East Antarctic ice sheet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2002 the Antarctic Peninsula's <a href="/wiki/Larsen_Ice_Shelf#Breakup" title="Larsen Ice Shelf">Larsen-B</a> ice shelf collapsed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jon_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jon-91">[88]</a></sup> In early 2008, about 570 km<sup>2</sup> (220 sq mi) of ice from the <a href="/wiki/Wilkins_Sound" title="Wilkins Sound">Wilkins Ice Shelf</a> on the southwest part of the peninsula collapsed, putting the remaining 15,000 km<sup>2</sup> (5,800 sq mi) of the ice shelf at risk. The ice was being held back by a "thread" of ice about 6 km (4 mi) wide,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[90]</a></sup> prior to its collapse in 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[91]</a></sup> As of 2022<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Antarctica&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the two most rapidly thinning ice shelves are those in front of the <a href="/wiki/Pine_Island_Glacier" title="Pine Island Glacier">Pine Island</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thwaites_Glacier" title="Thwaites Glacier">Thwaites glaciers</a>. Both ice shelves act to stabilise the glaciers that feed into them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[92]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ice_sheet_loss_and_sea_level_rise">Ice sheet loss and sea level rise</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_in_Antarctica" title="Climate change in Antarctica">Climate change in Antarctica</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:20201210_Antarctica_ice_mass_variation_-_NASA_GRACE-en.svg"><img alt="Ice loss accelerated between 2002 and 2021" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="750" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="210" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/20201210_Antarctica_ice_mass_variation_-_NASA_GRACE-en.svg/280px-20201210_Antarctica_ice_mass_variation_-_NASA_GRACE-en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/20201210_Antarctica_ice_mass_variation_-_NASA_GRACE-en.svg/420px-20201210_Antarctica_ice_mass_variation_-_NASA_GRACE-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/20201210_Antarctica_ice_mass_variation_-_NASA_GRACE-en.svg/560px-20201210_Antarctica_ice_mass_variation_-_NASA_GRACE-en.svg.png 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:20201210_Antarctica_ice_mass_variation_-_NASA_GRACE-en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ice mass loss since 2002</div></div></div>
<p>Antarctica contains about 90% of the world's ice. If all of this ice were melted, global sea levels would rise about 58 m (190 ft).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[93]</a></sup> In addition, Antarctica stores around 70% of global fresh water as ice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERiffenburgh2007128_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERiffenburgh2007128-97">[94]</a></sup> The continent is losing mass due to increased flow of its glaciers towards the ocean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-98">[95]</a></sup> The loss of mass from Antarctica's ice sheets is partially offset by additional snow falling back onto it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-99">[96]</a></sup> A 2018 <a href="/wiki/Systematic_review" title="Systematic review">systematic review</a> study estimated that ice loss across the entire continent was 43 <a href="/wiki/Tonne#Derived_units" title="Tonne">gigatonnes (Gt) per year</a> on average during the period from 1992 to 2002, but accelerated to an average of 220 Gt per year during the five years from 2012 to 2017.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IMBIE2018_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-IMBIE2018-100">[97]</a></sup> Antarctica's total contribution to sea level rise has been estimated to be 8 to 14 mm (0.31 to 0.55 in).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-99">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-98">[95]</a></sup>
</p><p>Most of the ice loss has taken place on the Antarctic Peninsula and West Antarctica.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[98]</a></sup> Estimates of the mass balance of the East Antarctic Ice Sheet as a whole range from slightly positive to slightly negative.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_98-2"><a href="#cite_note-:1-98">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[99]</a></sup> Increased ice outflow has been observed in some regions of East Antarctica, particularly at Wilkes Land.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_98-3"><a href="#cite_note-:1-98">[95]</a></sup>
</p><p>Future projections of ice loss depend on the speed of <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_mitigation" title="Climate change mitigation">climate change mitigation</a> and are uncertain. <a href="/wiki/Tipping_points_in_the_climate_system" title="Tipping points in the climate system">Tipping points</a> have been identified in some regions; when a certain threshold warming is reached, these regions may start melting at a significantly faster rate. If average temperatures were to begin to fall, the ice would not immediately be restored.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[100]</a></sup> A tipping point for the West Antarctic ice sheet is estimated to be between 1.5 and 2.0 °C (34.7 and 35.6 °F) of global warming. A full collapse would likely not take place unless warming reaches between 2 and 3 °C (36 and 37 °F), and may occur within centuries under pessimistic assumptions. This full collapse would lead to 2 to 5 meters (6.6 to 16.4 feet) of sea level rise. At <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7002276149999999999♠"></span>3 °C</span>, parts of the East Antarctic ice sheet are also projected to be fully lost, and total ice loss would lead to around 6 to 12 meters (20 to 39 feet) or more of sea level rise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[101]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ozone_depletion">Ozone depletion</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ozone_hole" title="Ozone hole">Ozone hole</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NASA_and_NOAA_Announce_Ozone_Hole_is_a_Double_Record_Breaker.png"><img alt="Image of the ozone hole spanning almost all of Antarctica" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="716" data-file-width="716" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/NASA_and_NOAA_Announce_Ozone_Hole_is_a_Double_Record_Breaker.png/220px-NASA_and_NOAA_Announce_Ozone_Hole_is_a_Double_Record_Breaker.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/NASA_and_NOAA_Announce_Ozone_Hole_is_a_Double_Record_Breaker.png/330px-NASA_and_NOAA_Announce_Ozone_Hole_is_a_Double_Record_Breaker.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/NASA_and_NOAA_Announce_Ozone_Hole_is_a_Double_Record_Breaker.png/440px-NASA_and_NOAA_Announce_Ozone_Hole_is_a_Double_Record_Breaker.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NASA_and_NOAA_Announce_Ozone_Hole_is_a_Double_Record_Breaker.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Image of the largest <a href="/wiki/Ozone_depletion" title="Ozone depletion">hole in the ozone layer</a> recorded, in September 2006</div></div></div>
<p>Scientists have studied the <a href="/wiki/Ozone_layer" title="Ozone layer">ozone layer</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmosphere</a> above Antarctica since the 1970s. In 1985, British scientists, working on data they had gathered at <a href="/wiki/Halley_Research_Station" title="Halley Research Station">Halley Research Station</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Brunt_Ice_Shelf" title="Brunt Ice Shelf">Brunt Ice Shelf</a>, discovered a large area of low <a href="/wiki/Ozone" title="Ozone">ozone</a> concentration over Antarctica.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schiermeier2009_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-schiermeier2009-106">[103]</a></sup> The 'ozone hole' covers almost the whole continent and was at its largest in September 2006;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-107">[104]</a></sup> the longest-lasting event occurred in 2020.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[105]</a></sup> The depletion is caused by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atmospheric_emissions" title="Atmospheric emissions">emission</a> of <a href="/wiki/Chlorofluorocarbon" title="Chlorofluorocarbon">chlorofluorocarbons</a> and halons into the atmosphere, which causes ozone to break down into other gases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[106]</a></sup> The extreme cold conditions of Antarctica allow <a href="/wiki/Polar_stratospheric_cloud" title="Polar stratospheric cloud">polar stratospheric clouds</a> to form. The clouds acts as <a href="/wiki/Catalysis" title="Catalysis">catalysts</a> for chemical reactions, which eventually lead to destruction of ozone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[107]</a></sup> The 1987 <a href="/wiki/Montreal_Protocol" title="Montreal Protocol">Montreal Protocol</a> has restricted the emissions of ozone-depleting substances. The ozone hole above Antarctica is predicted to slowly disappear; by the 2060s, levels of ozone are expected to have returned to values last recorded in the 1980s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WMO_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-WMO-111">[108]</a></sup>
</p><p>The ozone depletion can cause a cooling of around 6 °C (11 °F) in the <a href="/wiki/Stratosphere" title="Stratosphere">stratosphere</a>. The cooling strengthens the <a href="/wiki/Polar_vortex" title="Polar vortex">polar vortex</a>, and so prevents the outflow of the cold air near the South Pole, which in turn cools the continental mass of the East Antarctic ice sheet. The peripheral areas of Antarctica, especially the Antarctic Peninsula, are then subjected to higher temperatures, which accelerate the melting of the ice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schiermeier2009_106-1"><a href="#cite_note-schiermeier2009-106">[103]</a></sup> Models suggest that ozone depletion and the enhanced polar vortex effect may also account for the period of increasing sea ice extent, lasting from when observation started in the late 1970s until 2014. Since then, the coverage of Antarctic sea ice has decreased rapidly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40Year_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-40Year-112">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[110]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biodiversity">Biodiversity</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_realm" title="Antarctic realm">Antarctic realm</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_microorganism" title="Antarctic microorganism">Antarctic microorganism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Wildlife_of_Antarctica" title="Wildlife of Antarctica">Wildlife of Antarctica</a></div>
<p>Most species in Antarctica seem to be the descendants of species that lived there millions of years ago. As such, they must have survived multiple <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glacial_cycle" title="Glacial cycle">glacial cycles</a>. The species survived the periods of extremely cold climate in <a href="/wiki/Refugium_(population_biology)" title="Refugium (population biology)">isolated warmer areas</a>, such as those with <a href="/wiki/Geothermal_energy" title="Geothermal energy">geothermal heat</a> or areas that remained ice-free throughout the colder climate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[111]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Animals">Animals</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kaiserpinguine_mit_Jungen.jpg"><img alt="penguins with young" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1196" data-file-width="1820" decoding="async" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Kaiserpinguine_mit_Jungen.jpg/220px-Kaiserpinguine_mit_Jungen.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Kaiserpinguine_mit_Jungen.jpg/330px-Kaiserpinguine_mit_Jungen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Kaiserpinguine_mit_Jungen.jpg/440px-Kaiserpinguine_mit_Jungen.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kaiserpinguine_mit_Jungen.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Emperor_penguin" title="Emperor penguin">Emperor penguins</a> with juveniles</div></div></div>
<p>Invertebrate life of Antarctica includes species of microscopic <a href="/wiki/Mite" title="Mite">mites</a> such as <i><a href="/wiki/Alaskozetes_antarcticus" title="Alaskozetes antarcticus">Alaskozetes antarcticus</a></i>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lice" title="Lice">lice</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roundworm" title="Roundworm">nematodes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tardigrade" title="Tardigrade">tardigrades</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rotifer" title="Rotifer">rotifers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Krill" title="Krill">krill</a> and <a href="/wiki/Springtail" title="Springtail">springtails</a>. The few terrestrial <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vertebrates" title="Vertebrates">vertebrates</a> are limited to the sub-Antarctic islands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[112]</a></sup> The flightless <a href="/wiki/Midge" title="Midge">midge</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Belgica_antarctica" title="Belgica antarctica">Belgica antarctica</a></i>, the largest purely terrestrial animal in Antarctica, reaches <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1020198016">.mw-parser-output .frac{white-space:nowrap}.mw-parser-output .frac .num,.mw-parser-output .frac .den{font-size:80%;line-height:0;vertical-align:super}.mw-parser-output .frac .den{vertical-align:sub}.mw-parser-output .sr-only{border:0;clip:rect(0,0,0,0);height:1px;margin:-1px;overflow:hidden;padding:0;position:absolute;width:1px}</style>6 mm (<span class="frac" role="math"><span class="num">1</span>⁄<span class="den">4</span></span> in) in size.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[113]</a></sup>
