</p><p><a href="/wiki/Antarctic_krill" title="Antarctic krill">Antarctic krill</a>, which congregates in large <a href="/wiki/Shoaling_and_schooling" title="Shoaling and schooling">schools</a>, is the <a href="/wiki/Keystone_species" title="Keystone species">keystone species</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">ecosystem</a> of the Southern Ocean, being an important food organism for whales, seals, <a href="/wiki/Leopard_seal" title="Leopard seal">leopard seals</a>, fur seals, <a href="/wiki/Squid" title="Squid">squid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Notothenioidei" title="Notothenioidei">icefish</a>, and many bird species, such as penguins and <a href="/wiki/Albatross" title="Albatross">albatrosses</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrewby2002114_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrewby2002114-117">[114]</a></sup> Some species of marine animals exist and rely, directly or indirectly, on <a href="/wiki/Phytoplankton" title="Phytoplankton">phytoplankton</a>. Antarctic sea life includes <a href="/wiki/Penguin" title="Penguin">penguins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Blue_whale" title="Blue whale">blue whales</a>, <a href="/wiki/Orca" title="Orca">orcas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Colossal_squid" title="Colossal squid">colossal squids</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fur_seal" title="Fur seal">fur seals</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anc_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-Anc-118">[115]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_fur_seal" title="Antarctic fur seal">Antarctic fur seal</a> was very heavily hunted in the 18th and 19th centuries for its <a href="/wiki/Fur" title="Fur">pelt</a> by <a href="/wiki/Seal_hunting" title="Seal hunting">seal hunters</a> from the United States and the United Kingdom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[116]</a></sup> Leopard seals are <a href="/wiki/Apex_predator" title="Apex predator">apex predators</a> in the Antarctic ecosystem, and migrate across the Southern Ocean in search of food.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[117]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are approximately 40 bird species that breed on or close to Antarctica, including species of <a href="/wiki/Petrel" title="Petrel">petrels</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Penguins" title="Penguins">penguins</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cormorants" title="Cormorants">cormorants</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gulls" title="Gulls">gulls</a>. The ocean around Antarctica is visited by various other bird species, including some that normally reside in the Arctic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[118]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Emperor_penguin" title="Emperor penguin">emperor penguin</a> is the only penguin that breeds during the winter in Antarctica; it and the <a href="/wiki/Ad%C3%A9lie_penguin" title="Adélie penguin">Adélie penguin</a> breed farther south than any other penguin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anc_118-1"><a href="#cite_note-Anc-118">[115]</a></sup>
</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Census_of_Marine_Life" title="Census of Marine Life">Census of Marine Life</a> by some 500 researchers during the <a href="/wiki/International_Polar_Year" title="International Polar Year">International Polar Year</a>, was released in 2010. The research found that more than 235 marine organisms live in both polar regions, having bridged the gap of 12,000 km (7,456 mi). Large animals such as some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cetacean" title="Cetacean">cetaceans</a> and birds make the round trip annually. Smaller forms of life such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sea_cucumbers" title="Sea cucumbers">sea cucumbers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pteropoda" title="Pteropoda">free-swimming snails</a> also found in both polar oceans. Factors that may aid in their distribution include temperature differences between the deep ocean at the poles and the equator of no more than 5 °C (9 °F) and the major current systems or marine conveyor belt which are able to transport eggs and <a href="/wiki/Larva" title="Larva">larva</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[119]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fungi">Fungi</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Islas_Yalour_7,_l%C3%ADquen.jpg"><img alt="lichen photographed in Antarctica" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4211" data-file-width="2848" decoding="async" height="325" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Islas_Yalour_7%2C_l%C3%ADquen.jpg/220px-Islas_Yalour_7%2C_l%C3%ADquen.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Islas_Yalour_7%2C_l%C3%ADquen.jpg/330px-Islas_Yalour_7%2C_l%C3%ADquen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Islas_Yalour_7%2C_l%C3%ADquen.jpg/440px-Islas_Yalour_7%2C_l%C3%ADquen.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Islas_Yalour_7,_l%C3%ADquen.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orange_lichen" title="Orange lichen">Orange lichen</a> (<i>Caloplaca</i>) growing on the <a href="/wiki/Yalour_Islands" title="Yalour Islands">Yalour Islands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_Archipelago" title="Wilhelm Archipelago">Wilhelm Archipelago</a></div></div></div>
<p>About 1,150 species of <a href="/wiki/Fungus" title="Fungus">fungi</a> have been recorded in the Antarctic region, of which about 750 are non-<a href="/wiki/Lichen" title="Lichen">lichen</a>-forming.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-basplants_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-basplants-123">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[121]</a></sup> Some of the species, having evolved under extreme conditions, have <a href="/wiki/Endolith" title="Endolith">colonized structural cavities within porous rocks</a> and have contributed to shaping the rock formations of the McMurdo Dry Valleys and surrounding mountain ridges.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Selbmann_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-Selbmann-125">[122]</a></sup>
</p><p>The simplified <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(biology)" title="Morphology (biology)">morphology</a> of such fungi, along with their similar <a href="/wiki/Structure#Biological" title="Structure">biological structures</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metabolism_system" title="Metabolism system">metabolism systems</a> capable of remaining active at very low temperatures, and reduced life cycles, make them well suited to such environments. Their thick-walled and strongly <a href="/wiki/Melanin" title="Melanin">melanised</a> cells make them resistant to <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">UV</a> radiation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Selbmann_125-1"><a href="#cite_note-Selbmann-125">[122]</a></sup>
</p><p>The same features can be observed in algae and <a href="/wiki/Cyanobacteria" title="Cyanobacteria">cyanobacteria</a>, suggesting that they are <a href="/wiki/Adaptation" title="Adaptation">adaptations</a> to the conditions prevailing in Antarctica. This has led to speculation that <a href="/wiki/Life_on_Mars" title="Life on Mars">life on Mars</a> might have been similar to Antarctic fungi, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Cryomyces_antarcticus" title="Cryomyces antarcticus">Cryomyces antarcticus</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Cryomyces_minteri" title="Cryomyces minteri">Cryomyces minteri</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Selbmann_125-2"><a href="#cite_note-Selbmann-125">[122]</a></sup> Some of the species of fungi, which are apparently <a href="/wiki/Endemism" title="Endemism">endemic</a> to Antarctica, live in bird dung, and have evolved so they can grow inside extremely cold dung, but can also pass through the intestines of warm-blooded animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEde_Hoog2005vii_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEde_Hoog2005vii-126">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[124]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Plants">Plants</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flora_of_Antarctica" title="Flora of Antarctica">Flora of Antarctica</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Flora_Antarctica" title="Flora Antarctica">Flora Antarctica</a></div>
<p>Throughout its history, Antarctica has seen a wide variety of plant life. In the <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous" title="Cretaceous">Cretaceous</a>, it was dominated by a fern-<a href="/wiki/Conifer" title="Conifer">conifer</a> ecosystem, which changed into a <a href="/wiki/Rainforest" title="Rainforest">temperate rainforest</a> by the end of that period. During the colder <a href="/wiki/Neogene" title="Neogene">Neogene</a> (17–2.5 Ma), a <a href="/wiki/Tundra" title="Tundra">tundra</a> ecosystem replaced the rainforests. The climate of present-day Antarctica does not allow extensive vegetation to form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[125]</a></sup> A combination of freezing temperatures, poor soil quality, and a lack of moisture and sunlight inhibit plant growth, causing low <a href="/wiki/Species_diversity" title="Species diversity">species diversity</a> and limited distribution. The <a href="/wiki/Flora" title="Flora">flora</a> largely consists of <a href="/wiki/Bryophyte" title="Bryophyte">bryophytes</a> (25 species of <a href="/wiki/Marchantiophyta" title="Marchantiophyta">liverworts</a> and 100 species of <a href="/wiki/Moss" title="Moss">mosses</a>). There are three species of <a href="/wiki/Flowering_plant" title="Flowering plant">flowering plants</a>, all of which are found in the Antarctic Peninsula: <i><a href="/wiki/Deschampsia_antarctica" title="Deschampsia antarctica">Deschampsia antarctica</a></i> (Antarctic hair grass), <i><a href="/wiki/Colobanthus_quitensis" title="Colobanthus quitensis">Colobanthus quitensis</a></i> (Antarctic pearlwort) and the non-native <i><a href="/wiki/Poa_annua" title="Poa annua">Poa annua</a></i> (annual bluegrass).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[126]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_organisms">Other organisms</span></h3>
<p>Of the 700 species of algae in Antarctica, around half are marine phytoplankton. Multicoloured <a href="/wiki/Snow_algae" title="Snow algae">snow algae</a> are especially abundant in the coastal regions during the summer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[127]</a></sup> Bacteria have been found as deep as 800 m (0.50 mi) under the ice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20130206_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20130206-131">[128]</a></sup> It is thought to be likely that there exists a native bacterial community within the subterranean water body of Lake Vostok.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[129]</a></sup> The existence of life there is thought to strengthen the argument for the possibility of life on <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a>'s moon <a href="/wiki/Europa_(moon)" title="Europa (moon)">Europa</a>, which may have water beneath its water-ice crust.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[130]</a></sup> There exists a community of <a href="/wiki/Extremophile" title="Extremophile">extremophile</a> bacteria in the highly <a href="/wiki/Alkali" title="Alkali">alkaline</a> waters of <a href="/wiki/Lake_Untersee" title="Lake Untersee">Lake Untersee</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[132]</a></sup> The prevalence of highly resilient creatures in such inhospitable areas could further bolster the argument for <a href="/wiki/Extraterrestrial_life" title="Extraterrestrial life">extraterrestrial life</a> in cold, <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>-rich environments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-CloroxLake_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-CloroxLake-136">[133]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Conservation_and_environmental_protection">Conservation and environmental protection</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Antarctica,_pollution,_environment,_Russia,_Bellingshausen_1.JPG"><img alt="photograph of refuse on an island in Antarctica" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="863" data-file-width="1252" decoding="async" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Antarctica%2C_pollution%2C_environment%2C_Russia%2C_Bellingshausen_1.JPG/220px-Antarctica%2C_pollution%2C_environment%2C_Russia%2C_Bellingshausen_1.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Antarctica%2C_pollution%2C_environment%2C_Russia%2C_Bellingshausen_1.JPG/330px-Antarctica%2C_pollution%2C_environment%2C_Russia%2C_Bellingshausen_1.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Antarctica%2C_pollution%2C_environment%2C_Russia%2C_Bellingshausen_1.JPG/440px-Antarctica%2C_pollution%2C_environment%2C_Russia%2C_Bellingshausen_1.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Antarctica,_pollution,_environment,_Russia,_Bellingshausen_1.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Refuse littering the shoreline at <a href="/wiki/Bellingshausen_Station" title="Bellingshausen Station">Bellingshausen Station</a> on <a href="/wiki/King_George_Island_(South_Shetland_Islands)" title="King George Island (South Shetland Islands)">King George</a> Island, photographed in 1992</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wilhelmina_Bay_Antarctica_Humpback_Whale_6_(46421727295).jpg"><img alt="photograph of a whale in the Southern Ocean" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2566" data-file-width="3592" decoding="async" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Wilhelmina_Bay_Antarctica_Humpback_Whale_6_%2846421727295%29.jpg/220px-Wilhelmina_Bay_Antarctica_Humpback_Whale_6_%2846421727295%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Wilhelmina_Bay_Antarctica_Humpback_Whale_6_%2846421727295%29.jpg/330px-Wilhelmina_Bay_Antarctica_Humpback_Whale_6_%2846421727295%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Wilhelmina_Bay_Antarctica_Humpback_Whale_6_%2846421727295%29.jpg/440px-Wilhelmina_Bay_Antarctica_Humpback_Whale_6_%2846421727295%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wilhelmina_Bay_Antarctica_Humpback_Whale_6_(46421727295).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A whale in the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Ocean_Whale_Sanctuary" title="Southern Ocean Whale Sanctuary">Southern Ocean Whale Sanctuary</a></div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Agreed_Measures_for_the_Conservation_of_Antarctic_Fauna_and_Flora" title="Agreed Measures for the Conservation of Antarctic Fauna and Flora">first international agreement to protect Antarctica's biodiversity</a> was adopted in 1964.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-137">[134]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Overfishing" title="Overfishing">overfishing</a> of krill (an animal that plays a large role in the Antarctic ecosystem) led officials to enact regulations on fishing. The <a href="/wiki/Convention_for_the_Conservation_of_Antarctic_Marine_Living_Resources" title="Convention for the Conservation of Antarctic Marine Living Resources">Convention for the Conservation of Antarctic Marine Living Resources</a> (CCAMLR), an international treaty that came into force in 1980, regulates fisheries, aiming to preserve ecological relationships.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIAfactbook-People_1-4"><a href="#cite_note-CIAfactbook-People-1">[1]</a></sup> Despite these regulations, <a href="/wiki/Illegal,_unreported_and_unregulated_fishing" title="Illegal, unreported and unregulated fishing">illegal fishing</a>—particularly of the highly prized <a href="/wiki/Patagonian_toothfish" title="Patagonian toothfish">Patagonian toothfish</a> which is marketed as Chilean Sea Bass in the U.S.—remains a problem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[135]</a></sup>
</p><p>In analogy to the 1980 treaty on <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_fishery" title="Sustainable fishery">sustainable fishing</a>, countries led by New Zealand and the United States negotiated a treaty on mining. This <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Convention_on_the_Regulation_of_Antarctic_Mineral_Resources" title="Convention on the Regulation of Antarctic Mineral Resources">Convention on the Regulation of Antarctic Mineral Resources</a> was adopted in 1988. After a strong campaign from environmental organisations, first Australia and then France decided not to ratify the treaty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDay2019The_Antarctic_Treaty_of_1959_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDay2019The_Antarctic_Treaty_of_1959-139">[136]</a></sup> Instead, countries adopted the <a href="/wiki/Protocol_on_Environmental_Protection_to_the_Antarctic_Treaty" title="Protocol on Environmental Protection to the Antarctic Treaty">Protocol on Environmental Protection to the Antarctic Treaty</a> (the Madrid Protocol), which entered into force in 1998.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AUgovAntartica-Madrid_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-AUgovAntartica-Madrid-140">[137]</a></sup> The Madrid Protocol bans all mining, designating the continent as a "natural reserve devoted to peace and science".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[138]</a></sup>
</p><p>The pressure group <a href="/wiki/Greenpeace" title="Greenpeace">Greenpeace</a> established <a href="/wiki/World_Park_Base" title="World Park Base">a base on Ross Island</a> from 1987 to 1992, as part of its attempt to establish the continent as a <a href="/wiki/Transboundary_protected_area" title="Transboundary protected area">World Park</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[139]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Southern_Ocean_Whale_Sanctuary" title="Southern Ocean Whale Sanctuary">Southern Ocean Whale Sanctuary</a> was established in 1994 by the <a href="/wiki/International_Whaling_Commission" title="International Whaling Commission">International Whaling Commission</a>. It covers 50 million km<sup>2</sup> (19 million sq mi), and completely surrounds the Antarctic continent. All <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Commercial_whaling" title="Commercial whaling">commercial whaling</a> is banned in the zone, though Japan has continued to hunt whales in the area, ostensibly for research purposes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ASOC_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-ASOC-143">[140]</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite these protections, the biodiversity in Antarctica is still at risk from human activities. <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_Specially_Protected_Area" title="Antarctic Specially Protected Area">Specially protected areas</a> cover less than 2% of the area, and provide better protection for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charismatic_species" title="Charismatic species">animals with popular appeal</a> than for less visible animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_137-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-137">[134]</a></sup> There are more terrestrial protected areas than marine protected areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[141]</a></sup> Ecosystems are impacted by local and global threats, notably <a href="/wiki/Pollution" title="Pollution">pollution</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Invasive_species" title="Invasive species">invasion of non-native species</a>, and the various <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_in_Antarctica" title="Climate change in Antarctica">effects of climate change</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_137-2"><a href="#cite_note-:3-137">[134]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_exploration">History of exploration</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Antarctica" title="History of Antarctica">History of Antarctica</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Antarctic_expeditions" title="List of Antarctic expeditions">List of Antarctic expeditions</a>, <a href="/wiki/Women_in_Antarctica" title="Women in Antarctica">Women in Antarctica</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_polar_explorers" title="List of polar explorers">List of polar explorers</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/James_Cook" title="James Cook">Captain James Cook</a>'s ships, <a href="/wiki/HMS_Resolution_(1771)" title="HMS Resolution (1771)">HMS <i>Resolution</i></a> and <a href="/wiki/HMS_Adventure_(1771)" title="HMS Adventure (1771)"><i>Adventure</i></a>, crossed the Antarctic Circle on 17 January 1773, in December 1773, and again in January 1774.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERiffenburgh2007296_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERiffenburgh2007296-145">[142]</a></sup> Cook came within about 120 km (75 mi) of the Antarctic coast before retreating in the face of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Field_ice" title="Field ice">field ice</a> in January 1773.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdwards1999250_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdwards1999250-146">[143]</a></sup> In 1775, he called the existence of a polar continent "probable" and in another copy of his <a href="/wiki/General_journal" title="General journal">journal</a> he wrote: "[I] firmly believe it and it's more than probable that we have seen a part of it".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeaglehole1968643_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeaglehole1968643-147">[144]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="19th_century">19th century</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Atlas_pittoresque_pl_169.jpg"><img alt="picture of Adélie Land in 1840" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2199" data-file-width="2976" decoding="async" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Atlas_pittoresque_pl_169.jpg/220px-Atlas_pittoresque_pl_169.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Atlas_pittoresque_pl_169.jpg/330px-Atlas_pittoresque_pl_169.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Atlas_pittoresque_pl_169.jpg/440px-Atlas_pittoresque_pl_169.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Atlas_pittoresque_pl_169.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ad%C3%A9lie_Land" title="Adélie Land">Adélie Land</a>, depicted by <a href="/wiki/Jules_Dumont_d%27Urville" title="Jules Dumont d'Urville">Jules Dumont d'Urville</a> in his <i>Voyage au Pôle Sud</i> (1846)</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Seal_hunting" title="Seal hunting">Sealers</a> were among the earliest to go closer to the Antarctic landmass, perhaps in the earlier part of the 19th century. The oldest known human remains in the Antarctic region was a skull, dated from 1819 to 1825, that belonged to a young woman on <a href="/wiki/Yamana_Beach" title="Yamana Beach">Yamana Beach</a> at the <a href="/wiki/South_Shetland_Islands" title="South Shetland Islands">South Shetland Islands</a>. The woman, who was likely to have been part of a sealing expedition, was found in 1985.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[145]</a></sup>
</p><p>The first person to see Antarctica or its ice shelf was long thought to have been the British sailor <a href="/wiki/Edward_Bransfield" title="Edward Bransfield">Edward Bransfield</a>, a captain in the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">Royal Navy</a>, who discovered the tip of the Antarctic peninsula on 30 January 1820. However, a captain in the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Russian_Navy" title="Imperial Russian Navy">Imperial Russian Navy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fabian_Gottlieb_von_Bellingshausen" title="Fabian Gottlieb von Bellingshausen">Fabian Gottlieb von Bellingshausen</a> recorded seeing an ice shelf on 27 January.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrewby200239_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrewby200239-149">[146]</a></sup> The American sealer <a href="/wiki/Nathaniel_Palmer" title="Nathaniel Palmer">Nathaniel Palmer</a>, whose sealing ship was in the region at this time, may have been the first to sight the Antarctic Peninsula.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrewby2002139_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrewby2002139-150">[147]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/First_Russian_Antarctic_Expedition" title="First Russian Antarctic Expedition">First Russian Antarctic Expedition</a>, led by Bellingshausen and <a href="/wiki/Mikhail_Lazarev" title="Mikhail Lazarev">Mikhail Lazarev</a> on the 985-ton <a href="/wiki/Sloop-of-war" title="Sloop-of-war">sloop-of-war</a> <a href="/wiki/Vostok_(sloop-of-war)" title="Vostok (sloop-of-war)"><i>Vostok</i></a> and the 530-ton support vessel <a href="/wiki/Mirny_(sloop-of-war)" title="Mirny (sloop-of-war)"><i>Mirny</i></a>, reached a point within 32 km (20 mi) of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Queen_Maud%27s_Land" title="Queen Maud's Land">Queen Maud's Land</a> and recorded sighting an ice shelf at <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1073938472">.mw-parser-output .geo-default,.mw-parser-output .geo-dms,.mw-parser-output .geo-dec{display:inline}.mw-parser-output .geo-nondefault,.mw-parser-output .geo-multi-punct{display:none}.mw-parser-output .longitude,.mw-parser-output .latitude{white-space:nowrap}</style><span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//geohack.toolforge.org/geohack.php?pagename=Antarctica&amp;params=69_21_28_S_2_14_50_W_"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">69°21′28″S</span> <span class="longitude">2°14′50″W</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">﻿ / ﻿</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">69.35778°S 2.24722°W</span><span style="display:none">﻿ / <span class="geo">-69.35778; -2.24722</span></span></span></a></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tam_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tam-151">[148]</a></sup> on 27 January 1820,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[149]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[note 4]</a></sup> The sighting happened three days before Bransfield sighted the land of the <a href="/wiki/Trinity_Peninsula" title="Trinity Peninsula">Trinity Peninsula</a> of Antarctica, as opposed to the ice of an ice shelf, and 10 months before Palmer did so in November 1820. The first documented landing on Antarctica was by the American sealer <a href="/wiki/John_Davis_(sealer)" title="John Davis (sealer)">John Davis</a>, apparently at <a href="/wiki/Hughes_Bay" title="Hughes Bay">Hughes Bay</a> on 7 February 1821, although some historians dispute this claim, as there is no evidence Davis landed on the Antarctic continent rather than an offshore island.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[150]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJoyner19925_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJoyner19925-155">[151]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 22 January 1840, two days after the discovery of the coast west of the <a href="/wiki/Balleny_Islands" title="Balleny Islands">Balleny Islands</a>, some members of the crew of the 1837–1840 expedition of the French explorer <a href="/wiki/Jules_Dumont_d%27Urville" title="Jules Dumont d'Urville">Jules Dumont d'Urville</a> disembarked on the <a href="/wiki/Dumoulin_Islands" title="Dumoulin Islands">Dumoulin Islands</a>, off the coast of Adélie Land, where they took some mineral, algae, and animal samples, erected the French flag and claimed French <a href="/wiki/Territorial_claims_in_Antarctica" title="Territorial claims in Antarctica">sovereignty over the territory</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrewby200267_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrewby200267-156">[152]</a></sup> The American captain <a href="/wiki/Charles_Wilkes" title="Charles Wilkes">Charles Wilkes</a> led an expedition in 1838–1839, and was the first to claim he had discovered the continent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[153]</a></sup> the British naval officer <a href="/wiki/James_Clark_Ross" title="James Clark Ross">John Ross</a> failed to realise that what he referred to as "the various patches of land recently discovered by the English and American navigators on the verge of the Antarctic Circle" were connected to form a single continent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECawley2015131_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECawley2015131-158">[154]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAinsworth1847479_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAinsworth1847479-159">[155]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[note 5]</a></sup> The American explorer <a href="/wiki/Mercator_Cooper" title="Mercator Cooper">Mercator Cooper</a> landed on East Antarctica on 26 January 1853.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDay201388_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDay201388-163">[158]</a></sup>
</p><p>The first confirmed landing on the continental mass of Antarctica occurred in 1895, when the Norwegian-Swedish <a href="/wiki/Whaling" title="Whaling">whaling</a> ship <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_(ship)" title="Antarctic (ship)"><i>Antarctic</i></a> reached <a href="/wiki/Cape_Adare" title="Cape Adare">Cape Adare</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[159]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="20th_century">20th century</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TheSouthernParty_(cropped).jpg"><img alt="Shackleton and other explorers in Antarctica" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="919" data-file-width="1372" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/TheSouthernParty_%28cropped%29.jpg/220px-TheSouthernParty_%28cropped%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/TheSouthernParty_%28cropped%29.jpg/330px-TheSouthernParty_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/TheSouthernParty_%28cropped%29.jpg/440px-TheSouthernParty_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TheSouthernParty_(cropped).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Nimrod_Expedition" title="Nimrod Expedition"><i>Nimrod</i> Expedition</a> of 1907–1909 (<i>left to right</i>): <a href="/wiki/Frank_Wild" title="Frank Wild">Frank Wild</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Shackleton" title="Ernest Shackleton">Ernest Shackleton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eric_Marshall" title="Eric Marshall">Eric Marshall</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jameson_Adams" title="Jameson Adams">Jameson Adams</a></div></div></div>
<p>During the <i>Nimrod</i> Expedition led by the British explorer <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Shackleton" title="Ernest Shackleton">Ernest Shackleton</a> in 1907, parties led by <a href="/wiki/Edgeworth_David" title="Edgeworth David">Edgeworth David</a> became the first to climb Mount Erebus and to reach the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/South_Magnetic_Pole" title="South Magnetic Pole">South Magnetic Pole</a>. <a href="/wiki/Douglas_Mawson" title="Douglas Mawson">Douglas Mawson</a>, who assumed the leadership of the Magnetic Pole party on their perilous return, retired in 1931.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[160]</a></sup> Between December 1908 and February 1909: Shackleton and three members of his expedition became the first humans to traverse the Ross Ice Shelf, the first to cross the Transantarctic Mountains (via the Beardmore Glacier), and the first to set foot on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/South_Polar_Plateau" title="South Polar Plateau">South Polar Plateau</a>. On 14 December 1911, <a href="/wiki/Amundsen%27s_South_Pole_expedition" title="Amundsen's South Pole expedition">an expedition</a> led by Norwegian explorer <a href="/wiki/Roald_Amundsen" title="Roald Amundsen">Roald Amundsen</a> from the ship <i><a href="/wiki/Fram_(ship)" title="Fram (ship)">Fram</a></i> became the first to reach the geographic South Pole, using a route from the <a href="/wiki/Bay_of_Whales" title="Bay of Whales">Bay of Whales</a> and up the <a href="/wiki/Axel_Heiberg_Glacier" title="Axel Heiberg Glacier">Axel Heiberg Glacier</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERiffenburgh200730–32_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERiffenburgh200730–32-166">[161]</a></sup> One month later, the doomed <a href="/wiki/Terra_Nova_Expedition" title="Terra Nova Expedition">Scott Expedition</a> reached the pole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrewby2002159_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrewby2002159-167">[162]</a></sup>
</p><p>The American explorer <a href="/wiki/Richard_E._Byrd" title="Richard E. Byrd">Richard E. Byrd</a> led four expeditions to Antarctica during the 1920s, 1930s, and 1940s, using the first mechanised <a href="/wiki/Transport_in_Antarctica" title="Transport in Antarctica">tractors</a>. His expeditions conducted extensive geographical and scientific research, and he is credited with surveying a larger region of the continent than any other explorer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrewby200244_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrewby200244-168">[163]</a></sup> In 1937, <a href="/wiki/Ingrid_Christensen" title="Ingrid Christensen">Ingrid Christensen</a> became the first woman to step onto the Antarctic mainland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bla_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bla-169">[164]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Caroline_Mikkelsen" title="Caroline Mikkelsen">Caroline Mikkelsen</a> had landed on an island of Antarctica, earlier in 1935.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cam_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cam-170">[165]</a></sup>
</p><p>The South Pole was next reached on 31 October 1956, when a U.S. Navy group led by Rear Admiral <a href="/wiki/George_J._Dufek" title="George J. Dufek">George J. Dufek</a> successfully landed an aircraft there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[166]</a></sup> Six women were flown to the South Pole as a publicity stunt in 1969.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roy_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-Roy-172">[167]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[note 6]</a></sup> In the summer of 1996–1997, Norwegian explorer <a href="/wiki/B%C3%B8rge_Ousland" title="Børge Ousland">Børge Ousland</a> became the first person to cross Antarctica alone from coast to coast, helped by a kite on parts of the journey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[168]</a></sup> Ousland holds the record for the fastest unsupported journey to the South Pole, taking 34 days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[169]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Population">Population</span></h2>
<p>The first semi-permanent inhabitants of regions near Antarctica (areas situated south of the <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_Convergence" title="Antarctic Convergence">Antarctic Convergence</a>) were British and American sealers who used to spend a year or more on <a href="/wiki/South_Georgia_and_the_South_Sandwich_Islands" title="South Georgia and the South Sandwich Islands">South Georgia</a>, from 1786 onward. During the whaling era, which lasted until 1966, the population of the island varied from over 1,000 in the summer (over 2,000 in some years) to some 200 in the winter. Most of the whalers were Norwegian, with an increasing proportion from Britain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeadland1984238_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeadland1984238-176">[170]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[note 7]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AmundsenScottSuedpolStation.jpg"><img alt="photograph of the South Pole research station" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1012" data-file-width="1268" decoding="async" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/AmundsenScottSuedpolStation.jpg/220px-AmundsenScottSuedpolStation.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/AmundsenScottSuedpolStation.jpg/330px-AmundsenScottSuedpolStation.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/AmundsenScottSuedpolStation.jpg/440px-AmundsenScottSuedpolStation.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:AmundsenScottSuedpolStation.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The "ceremonial" <a href="/wiki/South_Pole#Ceremonial_South_Pole" title="South Pole">South Pole</a>, at <a href="/wiki/Amundsen%E2%80%93Scott_South_Pole_Station" title="Amundsen–Scott South Pole Station">Amundsen–Scott Station</a></div></div></div>
<p>The continent of Antarctica has never had a permanent resident population, although staffed <a href="/wiki/Research_stations_in_Antarctica" title="Research stations in Antarctica">research stations</a> are continuously maintained.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nat_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nat-178">[171]</a></sup> The number of people conducting and supporting scientific research and other work on the continent and its nearby islands varies from about 1,000 in winter to about 5,000 in the summer. Some of the <a href="/wiki/Research_station" title="Research station">research stations</a> are staffed year-round, the winter-over personnel typically arriving from their home countries for a one-year assignment. The <a href="/wiki/Russian_Orthodox_Church" title="Russian Orthodox Church">Russian Orthodox</a> <a href="/wiki/Trinity_Church_(Antarctica)" title="Trinity Church (Antarctica)">Holy Trinity Church</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Bellingshausen_Station" title="Bellingshausen Station">Bellingshausen Station</a> on <a href="/wiki/King_George_Island_(South_Shetland_Islands)" title="King George Island (South Shetland Islands)">King George Island</a> opened in 2004; it is manned year-round by one or two <a href="/wiki/Priest" title="Priest">priests</a>, who are similarly rotated every year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[172]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[173]</a></sup>
</p><p>The first child born in the southern polar region was a Norwegian girl, <a href="/wiki/Solveig_Gunbj%C3%B8rg_Jacobsen" title="Solveig Gunbjørg Jacobsen">Solveig Gunbjørg Jacobsen</a>, born in Grytviken on 8 October 1913.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeadland198412,_130_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeadland198412,_130-181">[174]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emilio_Marcos_Palma" title="Emilio Marcos Palma">Emilio Marcos Palma</a> was the first person born south of the <a href="/wiki/60th_parallel_south" title="60th parallel south">60th parallel south</a> and the first to be born on the Antarctic mainland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[175]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_Treaty_System" title="Antarctic Treaty System">Antarctic Treaty</a> prohibits any <a href="/wiki/Military_activity_in_the_Antarctic" title="Military activity in the Antarctic">military activity in Antarctica</a>, including the establishment of military bases and fortifications, military manoeuvres, and weapons testing. Military personnel or equipment are permitted only for scientific research or other peaceful purposes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[176]</a></sup> The only documented military land manoeuvre on the continent has been the small <a href="/wiki/Operaci%C3%B3n_90" title="Operación 90">Operation NINETY</a> by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Military_of_Argentina" title="Military of Argentina">Argentine military</a> in 1965.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[177]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Politics">Politics</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Senate_1960-img1.jpg"><img alt="photograph of the US signing the Antarctic Treay" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="742" data-file-width="950" decoding="async" height="172" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Senate_1960-img1.jpg/220px-Senate_1960-img1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Senate_1960-img1.jpg/330px-Senate_1960-img1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Senate_1960-img1.jpg/440px-Senate_1960-img1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Senate_1960-img1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The U.S. delegate <a href="/wiki/Herman_Phleger" title="Herman Phleger">Herman Phleger</a> signs the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antarctic_Treaty" title="Antarctic Treaty">Antarctic Treaty</a> in December 1959.</div></div></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/1539" title="1539">1539</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Monarchy_of_Spain" title="Monarchy of Spain">King of Spain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charles_V,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Charles V, Holy Roman Emperor">Charles V</a>, created the <a href="/wiki/Governorate_of_Terra_Australis" title="Governorate of Terra Australis">Governorate of Terra Australis</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oscar_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-Oscar-185">[178]</a></sup> granted to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pedro_Sancho_de_la_Hoz" title="Pedro Sancho de la Hoz">Pedro Sancho de la Hoz</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[179]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[180]</a></sup> who in 1540 transferred the title to the conqueror <a href="/wiki/Pedro_de_Valdivia" title="Pedro de Valdivia">Pedro de Valdivia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[181]</a></sup> Spain claimed all the territories to the south of the <a href="/wiki/Strait_of_Magellan" title="Strait of Magellan">Strait of Magellan</a> until the <a href="/wiki/South_Pole" title="South Pole">South Pole</a> and to the East and West the borders were the ones specified in the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Tordesillas" title="Treaty of Tordesillas">Treaty of Tordesillas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Zaragoza" title="Treaty of Zaragoza">Zaragoza</a> respectively. In 1555 the claim was incorporated to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Chile" title="Kingdom of Chile">Chile</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[182]</a></sup>
</p><p>Antarctica's status is regulated by the 1959 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antarctic_Treaty" title="Antarctic Treaty">Antarctic Treaty</a> and other related agreements, collectively called the Antarctic Treaty System. Antarctica is defined as all land and ice shelves south of 60° S for the purposes of the Treaty System.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIAfactbook-People_1-5"><a href="#cite_note-CIAfactbook-People-1">[1]</a></sup> The treaty was signed by twelve countries, including the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>, the United Kingdom, Argentina, <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chile</a>, Australia, and the United States. Since 1959, a further 42 countries have <a href="/wiki/Vienna_Convention_on_the_Law_of_Treaties#Signature,_ratification_and_accession" title="Vienna Convention on the Law of Treaties">acceeded</a> to the treaty. Countries can participate in decision-making if they can demonstrate that they do significant research on Antarctica; as of 2022<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Antarctica&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, 29 countries have this 'consultative status'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[183]</a></sup> Decisions are based on <a href="/wiki/Consensus_decision-making" title="Consensus decision-making">consensus</a>, instead of a <a href="/wiki/Voting" title="Voting">vote</a>. The treaty set aside Antarctica as a scientific preserve, and established freedom of scientific investigation and environmental protection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[184]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Territorial_claims">Territorial claims</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Governorate_of_Terra_Australis_(1539-1555),_Spain.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1219" data-file-width="744" decoding="async" height="360" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Governorate_of_Terra_Australis_%281539-1555%29%2C_Spain.svg/220px-Governorate_of_Terra_Australis_%281539-1555%29%2C_Spain.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Governorate_of_Terra_Australis_%281539-1555%29%2C_Spain.svg/330px-Governorate_of_Terra_Australis_%281539-1555%29%2C_Spain.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Governorate_of_Terra_Australis_%281539-1555%29%2C_Spain.svg/440px-Governorate_of_Terra_Australis_%281539-1555%29%2C_Spain.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Governorate_of_Terra_Australis_(1539-1555),_Spain.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of the Spanish <a href="/wiki/Governorate_of_Terra_Australis" title="Governorate of Terra Australis">governorate of Terra Australis</a> (1539-1555), the first territorial claim over the lands near the South Pole, later it was incorporated into the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Governorate_of_Chile" title="Governorate of Chile">Governorate of Chile</a>.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Territorial_claims_in_Antarctica" title="Territorial claims in Antarctica">Territorial claims in Antarctica</a></div><p>Sovereignty over regions of Antarctica is claimed by seven countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIAfactbook-People_1-6"><a href="#cite_note-CIAfactbook-People-1">[1]</a></sup> While a few of these countries have mutually recognised each other's claims,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVon_TigerstromLeane2005204_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVon_TigerstromLeane2005204-192">[185]</a></sup> the validity of the claims is not recognised universally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIAfactbook-People_1-7"><a href="#cite_note-CIAfactbook-People-1">[1]</a></sup> New claims on Antarctica have been suspended since 1959, although in 2015 Norway formally defined Queen Maud Land as including the unclaimed area between it and the South Pole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[186]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Argentine, British, and Chilean claims overlap, and have caused friction. In 2012, after the British <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Foreign_and_Commonwealth_Office" title="Foreign and Commonwealth Office">Foreign and Commonwealth Office</a> designated a previously unnamed area <a href="/wiki/Queen_Elizabeth_Land" title="Queen Elizabeth Land">Queen Elizabeth Land</a> in tribute to <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_II" title="Elizabeth II">Queen Elizabeth II</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diamond_Jubilee" title="Diamond Jubilee">Diamond Jubilee</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Foreign_&amp;_Commonwealth_Office_Press_Release_194-0"><a href="#cite_note-Foreign_&amp;_Commonwealth_Office_Press_Release-194">[187]</a></sup> the Argentinean government formally protested against the claim.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC_News:_Argentine_Protest_Against_Queen_Elizabeth_Land_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_News:_Argentine_Protest_Against_Queen_Elizabeth_Land-195">[188]</a></sup> The UK passed some of the area it claimed to Australia and New Zealand after they achieved independence. The claims by Britain, Australia, New Zealand, France, and Norway do not overlap, and are recognised by each other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVon_TigerstromLeane2005204_192-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVon_TigerstromLeane2005204-192">[185]</a></sup> Other members nations of the Antarctic Treaty do not recognize any claim, yet have shown some form of territorial interest in the past.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-peecug2_196-0"><a href="#cite_note-peecug2-196">[189]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><span class="flagicon"><img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="504" data-file-width="720" decoding="async" height="15" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/05/Flag_of_Brazil.svg/22px-Flag_of_Brazil.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/05/Flag_of_Brazil.svg/33px-Flag_of_Brazil.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/05/Flag_of_Brazil.svg/43px-Flag_of_Brazil.svg.png 2x" width="22"/> </span>Brazil has a designated '<a href="/wiki/Brazilian_Antarctica" title="Brazilian Antarctica">zone of interest</a>' that is not an actual claim.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morris1988_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-Morris1988-197">[190]</a></sup></li>
<li><span class="flagicon"><img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="900" decoding="async" height="15" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Flag_of_Peru.svg/23px-Flag_of_Peru.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Flag_of_Peru.svg/35px-Flag_of_Peru.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Flag_of_Peru.svg/45px-Flag_of_Peru.svg.png 2x" width="23"/> </span>Peru has formally reserved its right to make a claim.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-peecug2_196-1"><a href="#cite_note-peecug2-196">[189]</a></sup></li>
<li><span class="flagicon"><img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="900" decoding="async" height="15" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f3/Flag_of_Russia.svg/23px-Flag_of_Russia.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f3/Flag_of_Russia.svg/35px-Flag_of_Russia.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f3/Flag_of_Russia.svg/45px-Flag_of_Russia.svg.png 2x" width="23"/> </span>Russia has inherited the Soviet Union's right to claim territory under the original Antarctic Treaty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIAfactbook-US-RusClaim_198-0"><a href="#cite_note-CIAfactbook-US-RusClaim-198">[191]</a></sup></li>
<li><span class="flagicon"><img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="267" data-file-width="400" decoding="async" height="15" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Flag_of_South_Africa.svg/23px-Flag_of_South_Africa.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Flag_of_South_Africa.svg/35px-Flag_of_South_Africa.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Flag_of_South_Africa.svg/45px-Flag_of_South_Africa.svg.png 2x" width="23"/> </span>South Africa has formally reserved its right to make a claim.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-peecug2_196-2"><a href="#cite_note-peecug2-196">[189]</a></sup></li>
<li><span class="flagicon"><img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="650" data-file-width="1235" decoding="async" height="12" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a4/Flag_of_the_United_States.svg/23px-Flag_of_the_United_States.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a4/Flag_of_the_United_States.svg/35px-Flag_of_the_United_States.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a4/Flag_of_the_United_States.svg/46px-Flag_of_the_United_States.svg.png 2x" width="23"/> </span>United States reserved its right to make a claim in the original Antarctic Treaty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIAfactbook-US-RusClaim_198-1"><a href="#cite_note-CIAfactbook-US-RusClaim-198">[191]</a></sup></li></ul>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_activity">Human activity</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Tourism_in_Antarctica" title="Tourism in Antarctica">Tourism in Antarctica</a> and <a href="/wiki/Research_stations_in_Antarctica" title="Research stations in Antarctica">Research stations in Antarctica</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_activity_and_tourism">Economic activity and tourism</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Silversea_Silver_Cloud_Wilhelmina_Bay_Antarctica_2_(47284124562).jpg"><img alt="Photograph of a cruise ship off the Antarctic coast" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3600" data-file-width="5400" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Silversea_Silver_Cloud_Wilhelmina_Bay_Antarctica_2_%2847284124562%29.jpg/220px-Silversea_Silver_Cloud_Wilhelmina_Bay_Antarctica_2_%2847284124562%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Silversea_Silver_Cloud_Wilhelmina_Bay_Antarctica_2_%2847284124562%29.jpg/330px-Silversea_Silver_Cloud_Wilhelmina_Bay_Antarctica_2_%2847284124562%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Silversea_Silver_Cloud_Wilhelmina_Bay_Antarctica_2_%2847284124562%29.jpg/440px-Silversea_Silver_Cloud_Wilhelmina_Bay_Antarctica_2_%2847284124562%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Silversea_Silver_Cloud_Wilhelmina_Bay_Antarctica_2_(47284124562).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Cruise_ship" title="Cruise ship">cruise ship</a> <a href="/wiki/Silver_Cloud_(ship)" title="Silver Cloud (ship)">Silver Cloud</a> in <a href="/wiki/Wilhelmina_Bay" title="Wilhelmina Bay">Wilhelmina Bay</a></div></div></div>
<p>Deposits of coal, <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbons</a>, iron ore, <a href="/wiki/Platinum" title="Platinum">platinum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chromium" title="Chromium">chromium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nickel" title="Nickel">nickel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gold" title="Gold">gold</a> and other minerals have been found in Antarctica, but not in large enough quantities to extract.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[192]</a></sup> The Protocol on Environmental Protection to the Antarctic Treaty, which came into effect in 1998 and is due to be reviewed in 2048, restricts the exploitation of Antarctic resources, including minerals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[193]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Tourism" title="Tourism">Tourists</a> have been <a href="/wiki/Tourism_in_Antarctica" title="Tourism in Antarctica">visiting Antarctica</a> since 1957.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrewby2002187–188_201-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrewby2002187–188-201">[194]</a></sup> Tourism is subject to the provisions of the Antarctic Treaty and Environmental Protocol;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAATOTou_202-0"><a href="#cite_note-IAATOTou-202">[195]</a></sup> the self-regulatory body for the industry is the <a href="/wiki/International_Association_of_Antarctica_Tour_Operators" title="International Association of Antarctica Tour Operators">International Association of Antarctica Tour Operators</a> (IAATO).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrewby2002107_203-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrewby2002107-203">[196]</a></sup> Tourists arrive by small or medium ship at specific scenic locations with accessible concentrations of iconic wildlife.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrewby2002187–188_201-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrewby2002187–188-201">[194]</a></sup> Over 74,000 tourists visited the region during the 2019/2020 season, of which 18,500 travelled on cruise ships but did not leave them to explore on land.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAATOTou2_204-0"><a href="#cite_note-IAATOTou2-204">[197]</a></sup> The numbers of tourists fell rapidly after the start of the <a href="/wiki/COVID-19_pandemic" title="COVID-19 pandemic">COVID-19 pandemic</a>. Some nature conservation groups have expressed concern over the potential adverse effects caused by the influx of visitors, and have called for limits on the size of visiting <a href="/wiki/Cruise_ship" title="Cruise ship">cruise ships</a> and a tourism quota.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[198]</a></sup> The primary response by Antarctic Treaty parties has been to develop guidelines that set landing limits and closed or restricted zones on the more frequently visited sites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Secr_206-0"><a href="#cite_note-Secr-206">[199]</a></sup>
</p><p>Overland sightseeing flights operated out of Australia and New Zealand until the <a href="/wiki/Mount_Erebus_disaster" title="Mount Erebus disaster">Mount Erebus disaster</a> in 1979, when an <a href="/wiki/Air_New_Zealand" title="Air New Zealand">Air New Zealand</a> plane crashed into Mount Erebus, killing all of the 257 people aboard. <a href="/wiki/Qantas" title="Qantas">Qantas</a> resumed commercial overflights to Antarctica from Australia in the mid-1990s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDay2013507–509_207-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDay2013507–509-207">[200]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Research">Research</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:McMurdo-Station_aus_der_Vogelperspektive_(16481391425).jpg"><img alt="aerial photograph of McMurdo station" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="4000" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/McMurdo-Station_aus_der_Vogelperspektive_%2816481391425%29.jpg/220px-McMurdo-Station_aus_der_Vogelperspektive_%2816481391425%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/McMurdo-Station_aus_der_Vogelperspektive_%2816481391425%29.jpg/330px-McMurdo-Station_aus_der_Vogelperspektive_%2816481391425%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/McMurdo-Station_aus_der_Vogelperspektive_%2816481391425%29.jpg/440px-McMurdo-Station_aus_der_Vogelperspektive_%2816481391425%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:McMurdo-Station_aus_der_Vogelperspektive_(16481391425).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An aerial view of <a href="/wiki/McMurdo_Station" title="McMurdo Station">McMurdo Station</a>, the largest <a href="/wiki/Research_stations_in_Antarctica" title="Research stations in Antarctica">research station in Antarctica</a></div></div></div>
<p>In 2017, there were more than 4,400 scientists undertaking research in Antarctica, a number that fell to just over 1,100 in the winter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIAfactbook-People_1-8"><a href="#cite_note-CIAfactbook-People-1">[1]</a></sup> There are over 70 permanent and seasonal research stations on the continent; the largest, United States <a href="/wiki/McMurdo_Station" title="McMurdo Station">McMurdo Station</a>, is capable of housing more than 1,000 people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHund201441_208-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHund201441-208">[201]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[202]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/British_Antarctic_Survey" title="British Antarctic Survey">British Antarctic Survey</a> has five major research stations on Antarctica, one of which is completely portable. The Belgian <a href="/wiki/Princess_Elisabeth_Antarctica" title="Princess Elisabeth Antarctica">Princess Elisabeth station</a> is one of the most modern stations, and the first to be <a href="/wiki/Carbon_neutrality" title="Carbon neutrality">carbon-neutral</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarrollLopes2019160_210-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarrollLopes2019160-210">[203]</a></sup> Argentina, Australia, Chile and Russia also have a large scientific presence on Antarctica.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIAfactbook-People_1-9"><a href="#cite_note-CIAfactbook-People-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Geologist" title="Geologist">Geologists</a> primarily study <a href="/wiki/Plate_tectonics" title="Plate tectonics">plate tectonics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Meteorite" title="Meteorite">meteorites</a>, and the breakup of Gondwana. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glaciologist" title="Glaciologist">Glaciologists</a> study the history and dynamics of floating ice, <a href="/wiki/Snow" title="Snow">seasonal snow</a>, glaciers, and ice sheets. <a href="/wiki/Biologist" title="Biologist">Biologists</a>, in addition to researching the wildlife, are interested in how low temperatures and the presence of humans affect adaptation and survival strategies in organisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[204]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Biomedical_scientist" title="Biomedical scientist">Biomedical scientists</a> have made discoveries concerning the spreading of <a href="/wiki/Virus" title="Virus">viruses</a> and the body's response to extreme seasonal temperatures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[205]</a></sup></p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ALH84001_meteorite_Smithsonian.jpg"><img alt="Photograph of a meteorite found in Antarctica" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="369" data-file-width="355" decoding="async" height="229" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/ALH84001_meteorite_Smithsonian.jpg/220px-ALH84001_meteorite_Smithsonian.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/ALH84001_meteorite_Smithsonian.jpg/330px-ALH84001_meteorite_Smithsonian.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/35/ALH84001_meteorite_Smithsonian.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ALH84001_meteorite_Smithsonian.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An Antarctic <a href="/wiki/Meteorite" title="Meteorite">meteorite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Allan_Hills_84001" title="Allan Hills 84001">Allan Hills 84001</a> on display at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Smithsonian_Museum_of_Natural_History" title="Smithsonian Museum of Natural History">Smithsonian Museum of Natural History</a></div></div></div>
<p>The high elevation of the interior, the low temperatures, and the length of polar nights during the winter months all allow for better <a href="/wiki/Observational_astronomy" title="Observational astronomy">astronomical observations</a> at Antarctica than anywhere else on Earth. The view of space from Earth is improved by a thinner atmosphere at higher elevations, and a lack of water vapour in the atmosphere caused by freezing temperatures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bur_213-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bur-213">[206]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Astrophysics" title="Astrophysics">Astrophysicists</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Amundsen%E2%80%93Scott_South_Pole_Station" title="Amundsen–Scott South Pole Station">Amundsen–Scott South Pole Station</a> study <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmic_microwave_background_radiation" title="Cosmic microwave background radiation">cosmic microwave background radiation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neutrino" title="Neutrino">neutrinos</a> from space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[207]</a></sup> The largest <a href="/wiki/Neutrino_detector" title="Neutrino detector">neutrino detector</a> in the world, the <a href="/wiki/IceCube_Neutrino_Observatory" title="IceCube Neutrino Observatory">IceCube Neutrino Observatory</a>, is at the Amundsen-Scott station. It consists of around 5,500 <a href="/wiki/Optical_module" title="Optical module">digital optical modules</a>, some of which reach a depth of 2,450 m (8,040 ft), that are held in 1 km<sup>3</sup> (0.24 cu mi) of ice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-icecube_215-0"><a href="#cite_note-icecube-215">[208]</a></sup>
</p><p>Antarctica provides a unique environment for the study of <a href="/wiki/Meteorite" title="Meteorite">meteorites</a>: the dry polar desert preserves them well, and meteorites older than a million years have been found. They are relatively easy to find, as the dark stone meteorites stand out in a landscape of ice and snow, and the flow of ice accumulates them in certain areas. The <a href="/wiki/Adelie_Land_meteorite" title="Adelie Land meteorite">Adelie Land meteorite</a>, discovered in 1912, was the first to be found. Meteorites contain clues about the composition of the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a> and its early development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[209]</a></sup> Most meteorites come from asteroids, but a few meteorites found in Antarctica came from the Moon and Mars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[210]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[note 8]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>

<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-4"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-4">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The word was originally pronounced with the first <i>c</i> silent in English, but the <a href="/wiki/Spelling_pronunciation" title="Spelling pronunciation">spelling pronunciation</a> has become common and is often considered more correct. However, the pronunciation with a silent <i>c</i>, and even with the first <i>t</i> silent as well, is widespread and typical of many similar English words.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> The <i>c</i> had ceased to be pronounced in <a href="/wiki/Medieval_Latin" title="Medieval Latin">Medieval Latin</a> and was dropped from the spelling in <a href="/wiki/Old_French" title="Old French">Old French</a>, but it was added back for etymological reasons in English in the 17th century and thereafter began to be pronounced, but (as with other spelling pronunciations) at first only by less educated people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> For those who pronounce the first <i>t</i>, there is also variation between the pronunciations <i>Ant-ar(c)tica</i> and <i>An-tar(c)tica</i>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-14"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-14">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Before the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Ocean" title="Southern Ocean">Southern Ocean</a> was recognised as a separate ocean, it was considered to be surrounded by the southern <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific</a>, <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">Indian Oceans</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[12]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-49"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-49">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Geographical features, such as ice caps, are shown as they are today for identification purposes, not as how they appeared at these times.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-153"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-153">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The feature discovered by the Russians was the <a href="/wiki/Fimbul_Ice_Shelf" title="Fimbul Ice Shelf">Fimbul ice shelf</a>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-162"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-162">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Ross passed through what is now known as the Ross Sea and discovered Ross Island (both of which were named after him) in 1841. He sailed along a huge wall of ice that was later named the <a href="/wiki/Ross_Ice_Shelf" title="Ross Ice Shelf">Ross Ice Shelf</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrewby2002154_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrewby2002154-160">[156]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mount_Erebus" title="Mount Erebus">Mount Erebus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mount_Terror_(Antarctica)" title="Mount Terror (Antarctica)">Mount Terror</a> are named after two ships from his expedition: <a href="/wiki/HMS_Erebus_(1826)" title="HMS Erebus (1826)">HMS <i>Erebus</i></a> and <a href="/wiki/HMS_Terror_(1813)" title="HMS Terror (1813)"><i>Terror</i></a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrewby2002154,_185_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrewby2002154,_185-161">[157]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-173"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-173">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The women were Pam Young, Jean Pearson, <a href="/wiki/Lois_Jones_(scientist)" title="Lois Jones (scientist)">Lois Jones</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eileen_McSaveney" title="Eileen McSaveney">Eileen McSaveney</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Kay_Lindsay&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Kay Lindsay (page does not exist)">Kay Lindsay</a> and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Terry_Tickhill&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Terry Tickhill (page does not exist)">Terry Tickhill</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roy_172-1"><a href="#cite_note-Roy-172">[167]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-177"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-177">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The first settlements included <a href="/wiki/Grytviken" title="Grytviken">Grytviken</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leith_Harbour" title="Leith Harbour">Leith Harbour</a>, <a href="/wiki/King_Edward_Point" title="King Edward Point">King Edward Point</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stromness,_South_Georgia" title="Stromness, South Georgia">Stromness</a>, <a href="/wiki/Husvik" title="Husvik">Husvik</a>, <a href="/wiki/Prince_Olav_Harbour" title="Prince Olav Harbour">Prince Olav Harbour</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ocean_Harbour" title="Ocean Harbour">Ocean Harbour</a> and <a href="/wiki/Godthul" title="Godthul">Godthul</a>. Managers and other senior officers of the whaling stations often lived together with their families. Among them was the founder of Grytviken, Captain <a href="/wiki/Carl_Anton_Larsen" title="Carl Anton Larsen">Carl Anton Larsen</a>, a prominent Norwegian whaler and explorer who, along with his family, adopted British citizenship in 1910.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeadland1984238_176-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeadland1984238-176">[170]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-219"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-219">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Antarctician meteorites, particularly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ALH84001" title="ALH84001">ALH84001</a> discovered by <a href="/wiki/ANSMET" title="ANSMET">ANSMET</a>, were at the centre of the controversy about possible evidence of <a href="/wiki/Life_on_Mars#Meteorites" title="Life on Mars">life on Mars</a>. Because meteorites in space absorb and record cosmic radiation, the time elapsed since the meteorite hit the Earth can be calculated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-meteorite_218-0"><a href="#cite_note-meteorite-218">[211]</a></sup></span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>
